**George Washington** (February 22, 1732 [[O.S.](/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates "Old Style and New Style dates") February 11, 1731][[a]](#cite_note-fn1-1) – December 14, 1799) was a [Founding Father](/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States "Founding Fathers of the United States") and the first [president of the United States](/wiki/President_of_the_United_States "President of the United States"), serving from 1789 to 1797. As commander of the [Continental Army](/wiki/Continental_Army "Continental Army"), Washington led [Patriot](/wiki/Patriot_(American_Revolution) "Patriot (American Revolution)") forces to victory in the [American Revolutionary War](/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War "American Revolutionary War") against the [British Empire](/wiki/British_Empire "British Empire"). He is commonly known as the [Father of the Nation](/wiki/Father_of_the_Nation "Father of the Nation") for his role in bringing about [American independence](/wiki/American_independence "American independence").

Born in the [Colony of Virginia](/wiki/Colony_of_Virginia "Colony of Virginia"), Washington became the commander of the [Virginia Regiment](/wiki/Virginia_Regiment "Virginia Regiment") during the [French and Indian War](/wiki/French_and_Indian_War "French and Indian War") (1754–1763). He was later elected to the [Virginia House of Burgesses](/wiki/Virginia_House_of_Burgesses "Virginia House of Burgesses"), and opposed the perceived oppression of the American colonists by the British Crown. When the American Revolutionary War against the British began in 1775, Washington was appointed [commander-in-chief of the Continental Army](/wiki/Commanding_General_of_the_United_States_Army "Commanding General of the United States Army"). He directed a poorly organized and equipped force against disciplined British troops. Washington and his army achieved an early victory at the [Siege of Boston](/wiki/Siege_of_Boston "Siege of Boston") in March 1776 but were forced to [retreat from New York City](/wiki/New_York_and_New_Jersey_campaign "New York and New Jersey campaign") in November. Washington [crossed the Delaware River](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_crossing_of_the_Delaware_River "George Washington's crossing of the Delaware River") and won the battles [of Trenton](/wiki/Battle_of_Trenton "Battle of Trenton") in late 1776 and [of Princeton](/wiki/Battle_of_Princeton "Battle of Princeton") in early 1777, then lost the battles [of Brandywine](/wiki/Battle_of_Brandywine "Battle of Brandywine") and [of Germantown](/wiki/Battle_of_Germantown "Battle of Germantown") later that year. He faced criticism of his command, low troop morale, and a lack of provisions for his forces as the war continued. Ultimately Washington led a combined French and American force to a decisive victory over the British at [Yorktown](/wiki/Siege_of_Yorktown "Siege of Yorktown") in 1781. In the resulting [Treaty of Paris](/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783) "Treaty of Paris (1783)") in 1783, the British acknowledged the sovereign independence of the United States. Washington then served as president of the [Constitutional Convention](/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States) "Constitutional Convention (United States)") in 1787, which drafted the current [Constitution of the United States](/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States "Constitution of the United States").

Washington was unanimously elected the first U.S. president by the [Electoral College](/wiki/United_States_Electoral_College "United States Electoral College") in 1788 and 1792. He implemented a strong, well-financed national government while remaining impartial in the fierce rivalry that emerged within his cabinet between [Thomas Jefferson](/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson "Thomas Jefferson") and [Alexander Hamilton](/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton "Alexander Hamilton"). During the [French Revolution](/wiki/French_Revolution "French Revolution"), he proclaimed [a policy of neutrality](/wiki/Proclamation_of_Neutrality "Proclamation of Neutrality") while supporting the [Jay Treaty](/wiki/Jay_Treaty "Jay Treaty") with Britain. Washington set enduring precedents for the [office of president](/wiki/Executive_Office_of_the_President_of_the_United_States "Executive Office of the President of the United States"), including [republicanism](/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States "Republicanism in the United States"), a [peaceful transfer of power](/wiki/Peaceful_transfer_of_power "Peaceful transfer of power"), the use of the title "[Mr. President](/wiki/President_(government_title)#United_States "President (government title)")", and the [two-term tradition](/wiki/Twenty-second_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution#Background "Twenty-second Amendment to the United States Constitution"). [His 1796 farewell address](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_Farewell_Address "George Washington's Farewell Address") became a preeminent statement on republicanism: Washington wrote about the importance of national unity and the dangers that regionalism, partisanship, and foreign influence pose to it. As a planter of [tobacco](/wiki/Tobacco "Tobacco") and [wheat](/wiki/Wheat "Wheat") at [Mount Vernon](/wiki/Mount_Vernon "Mount Vernon"), Washington [owned many slaves](/wiki/George_Washington_and_slavery "George Washington and slavery"). He began opposing slavery near the end of his life, and provided in his will for the eventual [manumission](/wiki/Manumission "Manumission") of his slaves.

Washington's image is an icon of [American culture](/wiki/American_culture "American culture") and he [has been extensively memorialized](/wiki/List_of_memorials_to_George_Washington "List of memorials to George Washington"). His namesakes include [the national capital](/wiki/Washington,_D.C. "Washington, D.C.") and the [State of Washington](/wiki/State_of_Washington "State of Washington"). In both popular and scholarly polls, he is consistently considered one of the greatest presidents in American history.

## Early life (1732–1752)

Further information: [Washington family](/wiki/Washington_family "Washington family")

| * [v](/wiki/Template:George_Washington_series "Template:George Washington series") * [t](/wiki/Template_talk:George_Washington_series "Template talk:George Washington series") * [e](/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:George_Washington_series "Special:EditPage/Template:George Washington series") |

George Washington was born on February 22, 1732,[[a]](#cite_note-fn1-1) at [Popes Creek](/wiki/George_Washington_Birthplace_National_Monument "George Washington Birthplace National Monument") in [Westmoreland County, Virginia](/wiki/Westmoreland_County,_Virginia "Westmoreland County, Virginia").[[3]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow20103–6-4) He was the first of six children of [Augustine](/wiki/Augustine_Washington "Augustine Washington") and [Mary Ball Washington](/wiki/Mary_Ball_Washington "Mary Ball Washington").[[4]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling20023Chernow20105–7-5) His father was a [justice of the peace](/wiki/Justice_of_the_peace "Justice of the peace") and a prominent public figure who had four additional children from his first marriage to Jane Butler.[[5]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling20099Chernow20106–8-6) Washington was not close to his father and rarely mentioned him in later years; he had a fractious relationship with his mother.[[6]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling20106–7Rhodehamel20172:_Powerful_Ambitions,_Powerful_Friends-7) Among his siblings, he was particularly close to his older half-brother [Lawrence](/wiki/Lawrence_Washington_(1718%E2%80%931752) "Lawrence Washington (1718–1752)").[[7]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELengel20057-8)

The family moved to a plantation on [Little Hunting Creek](/wiki/Little_Hunting_Creek "Little Hunting Creek") in 1735 before settling at [Ferry Farm](/wiki/Ferry_Farm "Ferry Farm") near [Fredericksburg, Virginia](/wiki/Fredericksburg,_Virginia "Fredericksburg, Virginia"), in 1738. When Augustine died in 1743, Washington inherited Ferry Farm and ten slaves; Lawrence inherited Little Hunting Creek and renamed it [Mount Vernon](/wiki/Mount_Vernon "Mount Vernon").[[8]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevy201339,_55,_56Morgan2005407-9) Because of his father's death, Washington did not have the formal education his elder half-brothers had received at [Appleby Grammar School](/wiki/Appleby_Grammar_School "Appleby Grammar School") in England; he instead attended the [Lower Church](/wiki/Lower_Church "Lower Church") School in [Hartfield](/wiki/Hartfield,_Virginia "Hartfield, Virginia"). He learned mathematics and land [surveying](/wiki/Surveying "Surveying"), and became a talented [draftsman](/wiki/Draftsman "Draftsman") and [mapmaker](/wiki/Cartography "Cartography"). By early adulthood, he was writing with what his biographer [Ron Chernow](/wiki/Ron_Chernow "Ron Chernow") described as "considerable force" and "precision".[[9]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201010–12Ferling200214Ferling20105–6-10) As a teenager, Washington compiled over a hundred rules for social interaction styled *The Rules of Civility*, copied from an English translation of a French guidebook.[[10]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison201519-11)

Washington often visited [Belvoir](/wiki/Belvoir_(plantation) "Belvoir (plantation)"), the plantation of [William Fairfax](/wiki/William_Fairfax "William Fairfax"), Lawrence's father-in-law, and Mount Vernon. Fairfax became Washington's patron and surrogate father. In 1748, Washington spent a month with a team surveying Fairfax's [Shenandoah Valley](/wiki/Shenandoah_Valley "Shenandoah Valley") property.[[11]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201010,_19Ferling200214–15Randall199736-12) The following year, he received a surveyor's license from the [College of William & Mary](/wiki/College_of_William_%26_Mary "College of William & Mary").[[b]](#cite_note-14) Even though Washington had not served the customary [apprenticeship](/wiki/Apprenticeship "Apprenticeship"), [Thomas Fairfax](/wiki/Thomas_Fairfax,_6th_Lord_Fairfax_of_Cameron "Thomas Fairfax, 6th Lord Fairfax of Cameron") (William's cousin) appointed him surveyor of [Culpeper County, Virginia](/wiki/Culpeper_County,_Virginia "Culpeper County, Virginia"). Washington took his oath of office on July 20, 1749, and resigned in 1750.[[12]](#cite_note-professional-surveys-13) By 1752, he had bought almost 1,500 acres (600 ha) in the Shenandoah Valley and owned 2,315 acres (937 ha).[[13]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936v._19,_p._510Chernow201022–23-15)

In 1751, Washington left mainland North America for the first and only time, when he accompanied Lawrence to [Barbados](/wiki/Barbados "Barbados"), hoping the climate would cure his brother's [tuberculosis](/wiki/Tuberculosis "Tuberculosis").[[14]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201024Rhodehamel20172:_Powerful_Ambitions,_Powerful_Friends-16) Washington contracted [smallpox](/wiki/Smallpox "Smallpox") during the trip, which left his face slightly scarred.[[15]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhodehamel20172:_Powerful_Ambitions,_Powerful_Friends-17) Lawrence died in 1752, and Washington leased Mount Vernon from his widow, Ann; he inherited it outright after her death in 1761.[[16]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201026,_52,_98-18)

## Colonial military career (1752–1758)

Lawrence Washington's service as adjutant general of the [Virginia militia](/wiki/Virginia_militia "Virginia militia") inspired George to seek a [militia commission](/wiki/Commission_(document) "Commission (document)"). Virginia's lieutenant governor, [Robert Dinwiddie](/wiki/Robert_Dinwiddie "Robert Dinwiddie"), appointed Washington as a major and commander of one of the four militia districts. The British and French were competing for control of the [Ohio River Valley](/wiki/Ohio_River "Ohio River"): the British were constructing forts along the river, and the French between the river and [Lake Erie](/wiki/Lake_Erie "Lake Erie").[[17]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson200731–32Chernow201026–27,_31-19)

In October 1753, Dinwiddie appointed Washington as a [special envoy](/wiki/Diplomatic_rank#Special_envoy "Diplomatic rank") to demand the French forces vacate land that was claimed by the British. Washington was also directed to make peace with the [Iroquois Confederacy](/wiki/Iroquois_Confederacy "Iroquois Confederacy") and to gather intelligence about the French forces.[[18]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200915–16-20) Washington met with Iroquois leader [Tanacharison](/wiki/Tanacharison "Tanacharison") at [Logstown](/wiki/Logstown "Logstown").[[19]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhodehamel20173:_War_for_North_America-21) Washington said that at this meeting Tanacharison named him [Conotocaurius](/wiki/Town_Destroyer "Town Destroyer"). This name, meaning "devourer of villages", had previously been given to his great-grandfather [John Washington](/wiki/John_Washington "John Washington") in the late 17th century by the [Susquehannock](/wiki/Susquehannock "Susquehannock").[[20]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalloway201825,_69-22)

Washington's party reached the Ohio River in November 1753 and was intercepted by a French patrol. The party was escorted to [Fort Le Boeuf](/wiki/Fort_Le_Boeuf "Fort Le Boeuf"), where Washington was received in a friendly manner. He delivered the British demand to vacate to the French commander [Jacques Legardeur de Saint-Pierre](/wiki/Jacques_Legardeur_de_Saint-Pierre "Jacques Legardeur de Saint-Pierre"), but the French refused to leave. Saint-Pierre gave Washington his official answer after a few days' delay, as well as food and winter clothing for his party's journey back to Virginia.[[21]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200915–18Lengel200523–24Randall199774Chernow201026–27,_31-23) Washington completed the precarious mission in difficult winter conditions, achieving a measure of distinction when his report was published in Virginia and London.[[22]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison201525–26Ferling200915–18-24)

### French and Indian War

Main article: [George Washington in the French and Indian War](/wiki/George_Washington_in_the_French_and_Indian_War "George Washington in the French and Indian War")

In February 1754, Dinwiddie promoted Washington to lieutenant colonel and second-in-command of the 300-strong [Virginia Regiment](/wiki/Virginia_Regiment "Virginia Regiment"), with orders to confront the French at the [Forks of the Ohio](/wiki/Point_State_Park "Point State Park").[[23]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201031–32Ferling200918–19-25) Washington set out with half the regiment in April and was soon aware that a French force of 1,000 had begun construction of [Fort Duquesne](/wiki/Fort_Duquesne "Fort Duquesne") there. In May, having established a [defensive position](/wiki/Fort_Necessity_National_Battlefield "Fort Necessity National Battlefield") at Great Meadows, Washington learned that the French had made camp seven miles (11 km) away; he decided to take the offensive.[[24]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201041–42-26) The French detachment proved to be only about 50 men, so on May 28 Washington commanded [an ambush](/wiki/Battle_of_Jumonville_Glen "Battle of Jumonville Glen"). His small force of Virginians and Indian allies[[c]](#cite_note-28)[[26]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201042-29) killed the French, including their commander [Joseph Coulon de Jumonville](/wiki/Joseph_Coulon_de_Jumonville "Joseph Coulon de Jumonville"), who had been carrying a diplomatic message for the British. The French later found their countrymen dead and [scalped](/wiki/Scalped "Scalped"), blaming Washington, who had retreated to [Fort Necessity](/wiki/Fort_Necessity "Fort Necessity").[[27]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200924–25Rhodehamel20173:_War_for_North_America-30)

The rest of the Virginia Regiment joined Washington the following month with news that he had been promoted to the rank of colonel and given command of the full regiment. They were reinforced by an [independent company of a hundred South Carolinians](/wiki/British_Army_Independent_Companies_in_South_Carolina "British Army Independent Companies in South Carolina") led by Captain [James Mackay](/wiki/James_Mackay_(British_Army_officer) "James Mackay (British Army officer)"); his royal commission outranked Washington's and a conflict of command ensued. On July 3, 900 French soldiers attacked Fort Necessity, and the [ensuing battle](/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Necessity "Battle of Fort Necessity") ended in Washington's surrender.[[28]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200923–25Ellis200415–17-31) Washington did not speak French, but signed a surrender document in which he unwittingly took responsibility for "assassinating" Jumonville, later blaming the translator for not properly translating it.[[29]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200924–25Chernow201042–45-32) The Virginia Regiment was divided and Washington was offered a captaincy in one of the newly formed regiments. He refused, as it would have been a demotion—the British had ordered that "colonials" could not be ranked any higher than captain—and instead resigned his commission.[[30]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200926-33)[[31]](#cite_note-anb-34) The Jumonville affair became the incident which ignited the [French and Indian War](/wiki/French_and_Indian_War "French and Indian War").[[32]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200919–24Ellis200413Coe202019-35)

In 1755, Washington volunteered as an aide to General [Edward Braddock](/wiki/Edward_Braddock "Edward Braddock"), who led a British [expedition](/wiki/Braddock_Expedition "Braddock Expedition") to expel the French from Fort Duquesne and the [Ohio Country](/wiki/Ohio_Country "Ohio Country").[[33]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201053-36) On Washington's recommendation, Braddock split the army into one main column and a smaller "flying column".[[34]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden199637Ferling201035–36-37) Washington was suffering from severe [dysentery](/wiki/Dysentery "Dysentery") so did not initially travel with the expedition forces. When he rejoined Braddock at Monongahela, still very ill, the French and their Indian allies ambushed the divided army. Two-thirds of the British force became casualties in the ensuing [Battle of the Monongahela](/wiki/Battle_of_the_Monongahela "Battle of the Monongahela"), and Braddock was killed. Under the command of Lieutenant Colonel [Thomas Gage](/wiki/Thomas_Gage "Thomas Gage"), Washington rallied the survivors and formed a [rear guard](/wiki/Rear_guard "Rear guard"), allowing the remnants of the force to retreat.[[35]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden199637–46Ferling201035–36Chernow201057–58-38) During the engagement, Washington had two horses shot out from under him, and his hat and coat were pierced by bullets.[[36]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhodehamel20174:_The_Rise_of_George_Washington-39) His conduct redeemed his reputation among critics of his command in the Battle of Fort Necessity,[[37]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200928–30-40) but he was not included by the succeeding commander (Colonel Thomas Dunbar) in planning subsequent operations.[[38]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden199637–46-41)

The Virginia Regiment was reconstituted in August 1755, and Dinwiddie appointed Washington its commander, again with the rank of colonel. Washington clashed over seniority almost immediately, this time with Captain [John Dagworthy](/wiki/John_Dagworthy "John Dagworthy"), who commanded a detachment of Marylanders at the regiment's headquarters in [Fort Cumberland](/wiki/Fort_Cumberland_(Maryland) "Fort Cumberland (Maryland)").[[39]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200424Ferling200930–31-42) Washington, impatient for an offensive against Fort Duquesne, was convinced Braddock would have granted him a royal commission and pressed his case in February 1756 with Braddock's successor as [Commander-in-Chief](/wiki/Commander-in-Chief,_North_America "Commander-in-Chief, North America"), [William Shirley](/wiki/William_Shirley "William Shirley"), and again in January 1757 with Shirley's successor, [Lord Loudoun](/wiki/John_Campbell,_4th_Earl_of_Loudoun "John Campbell, 4th Earl of Loudoun"). Loudoun humiliated Washington, refused him a royal commission, and agreed only to relieve him of the responsibility of manning Fort Cumberland.[[40]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200931–32,_38–39-43)

In 1758, the Virginia Regiment was assigned to the British [Forbes Expedition](/wiki/Forbes_Expedition "Forbes Expedition") to capture Fort Duquesne.[[41]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1965194-44)[[31]](#cite_note-anb-34) General [John Forbes](/wiki/John_Forbes_(British_Army_officer) "John Forbes (British Army officer)") took Washington's advice on some aspects of the expedition but rejected his opinion on the best route to the fort.[[42]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200431–32Misencik2014176-45) Forbes nevertheless made Washington a [brevet](/wiki/Brevet_(military) "Brevet (military)") brigadier general and gave him command of one of the three brigades that was assigned to assault the fort. The French had abandoned the fort and the valley before the assault, however, and Washington only saw a [friendly fire](/wiki/Friendly_fire "Friendly fire") incident, which left 14 dead and 26 injured. Frustrated, he resigned his commission soon afterwards and returned to Mount Vernon.[[43]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200943Chernow201090–91Lengel200575–76,_81-46)

Under Washington, the Virginia Regiment had defended 300 miles (480 km) of frontier against twenty Indian attacks in ten months.[[44]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitzpatrick1936511–512Flexner1965138Fischer200415–16Ellis200438-47) He increased the professionalism of the regiment as it grew from 300 to 1,000 men. Though he failed to realize a royal commission, which made him hostile towards the British,[[31]](#cite_note-anb-34) he gained self-confidence, leadership skills, and knowledge of British military tactics. The destructive competition Washington witnessed among colonial politicians fostered his later support of a strong central government.[[45]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201092–93Ferling200232–33-48)

## Marriage, civilian and political life (1759–1775)

Main article: [George Washington's political evolution](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_political_evolution "George Washington's political evolution")

*Colonel George Washington*, a 1772 portrait of Washington by [Charles Willson Peale](/wiki/Charles_Willson_Peale "Charles Willson Peale")

*Martha Dandridge Custis*, a 1757 portrait of [Martha Washington](/wiki/Martha_Washington "Martha Washington") by [John Wollaston](/wiki/John_Wollaston_(painter) "John Wollaston (painter)")

On January 6, 1759, Washington, at age 26, married [Martha Dandridge Custis](/wiki/Martha_Washington "Martha Washington"), the 27-year-old widow of wealthy plantation owner [Daniel Parke Custis](/wiki/Daniel_Parke_Custis "Daniel Parke Custis"). Martha was intelligent, gracious, and experienced in managing a planter's estate, and the couple had a happy marriage.[[46]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200233–34Wiencek200369-49) They lived at Mount Vernon, where Washington cultivated tobacco and wheat.[[47]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201097–98Fischer200414-50) The marriage gave Washington control over Martha's one-third [dower](/wiki/Dower "Dower") interest in the 18,000-acre (7,300 ha) [Custis estate](/wiki/Daniel_Parke_Custis#Estate "Daniel Parke Custis"), and he managed the remaining two-thirds for Martha's children. As a result, he became one of the wealthiest men in Virginia, which increased his social standing.[[48]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiencek20039–10,_67–69,_80–81-51)

At Washington's urging, Governor [Lord Botetourt](/wiki/Norborne_Berkeley,_4th_Baron_Botetourt "Norborne Berkeley, 4th Baron Botetourt") fulfilled Dinwiddie's 1754 promise to grant land bounties to those who served with volunteer militias during the French and Indian War.[[49]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERasmussenTilton1999100Chernow2010184-52) In late 1770, Washington inspected the lands in the Ohio and [Great Kanawha](/wiki/Kanawha_River "Kanawha River") regions, and he engaged surveyor [William Crawford](/wiki/William_Crawford_(soldier) "William Crawford (soldier)") to subdivide it. Crawford allotted 23,200 acres (9,400 ha) to Washington, who told the veterans that their land was unsuitable for farming and agreed to purchase 20,147 acres (8,153 ha), leaving some feeling that they had been duped.[[50]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200244–45Grizzard2002135–137-53) He also doubled the size of Mount Vernon to 6,500 acres (2,600 ha) and, by 1775, had more than doubled its slave population to over one hundred.[[51]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200441–42,_48-54)

As a respected military hero and large landowner, Washington held local offices and was elected to the Virginia provincial legislature, representing [Frederick County](/wiki/Frederick_County,_Virginia "Frederick County, Virginia") in the [Virginia House of Burgesses](/wiki/Virginia_House_of_Burgesses "Virginia House of Burgesses") for seven years beginning in 1758.[[d]](#cite_note-56)[[51]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200441–42,_48-54) Early in his legislative career, Washington rarely spoke at or even attended legislative sessions, but was more politically active starting in the 1760s, becoming a prominent critic of Britain's taxation and [mercantilist](/wiki/Mercantilist "Mercantilist") policies towards the American colonies.[[53]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200949–54,_68-57) Washington imported luxury goods from England, paying for them by exporting tobacco. His profligate spending combined with low tobacco prices left him £1,800 in debt by 1764.[[54]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200449–50-58) Washington's complete reliance on London tobacco buyer and merchant Robert Cary also threatened his economic security.[[e]](#cite_note-60)[[56]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200451-61) Between 1764 and 1766, he sought to diversify his holdings: he changed Mount Vernon's primary cash crop from tobacco to wheat and expanded operations to include [flour milling](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_Gristmill "George Washington's Gristmill") and [hemp farming](/wiki/Hemp_farming "Hemp farming").[[57]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010141Ragsdale202123,_41–42-62) Washington's stepdaughter Patsy suffered from [epileptic](/wiki/Epilepsy "Epilepsy") attacks, and she died at Mount Vernon in 1773, allowing Washington to use part of the inheritance from her estate to settle his debts.[[58]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010161Gardner2013-63)

### Opposition to the British Parliament and Crown

Further information: [American Revolution](/wiki/American_Revolution "American Revolution") and [George Washington in the American Revolution](/wiki/George_Washington_in_the_American_Revolution "George Washington in the American Revolution")

Washington was opposed to the taxes which the [British Parliament](/wiki/British_Parliament "British Parliament") imposed on the Colonies without [proper representation](/wiki/Virtual_representation "Virtual representation").[[59]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010136-64) He believed the [Stamp Act 1765](/wiki/Stamp_Act_1765 "Stamp Act 1765") was oppressive and celebrated its repeal the following year. In response to the [Townshend Acts](/wiki/Townshend_Acts "Townshend Acts"), he introduced a proposal in May 1769 which urged Virginians to boycott British goods; the Townshend Acts were mostly repealed in 1770.[[60]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlover201442–46Taylor201675-65) Washington and other colonists were also angered by the [Royal Proclamation of 1763](/wiki/Royal_Proclamation_of_1763 "Royal Proclamation of 1763") (which banned American settlement west of the [Allegheny Mountains](/wiki/Allegheny_Mountains "Allegheny Mountains"))[[61]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010137,_148Taylor201661,_75-66) and British interference in American western land [speculation](/wiki/Speculation "Speculation") (in which Washington was a participant).[[62]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalloway2018184-67)

Parliament sought to punish Massachusetts colonists for their role in the [Boston Tea Party](/wiki/Boston_Tea_Party "Boston Tea Party") in 1774 by passing the [Coercive Acts](/wiki/Intolerable_Acts "Intolerable Acts"), which Washington saw as "an invasion of our rights and privileges".[[63]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997262Chernow2010166Taylor2016119-68) That July, he and [George Mason](/wiki/George_Mason "George Mason") drafted a [list of resolutions](/wiki/Fairfax_Resolves "Fairfax Resolves") for the Fairfax County committee, including a call to end the [Atlantic slave trade](/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade "Atlantic slave trade"); the resolutions were adopted.[[64]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010167-69) In August, Washington attended the [First Virginia Convention](/wiki/Virginia_Conventions "Virginia Conventions") and was selected as a delegate to the [First Continental Congress](/wiki/First_Continental_Congress "First Continental Congress").[[65]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2010100Glenn201482-70) As tensions rose in 1774, he helped train militias in Virginia and organized enforcement of the [Continental Association](/wiki/Continental_Association "Continental Association") boycott of British goods instituted by the Congress.[[66]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2010108Taylor2016126–127-71)

## Commander in chief of the army (1775–1783)

Further information: [Military career of George Washington](/wiki/Military_career_of_George_Washington "Military career of George Washington")

The [American Revolutionary War](/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War "American Revolutionary War") broke out on April 19, 1775, with the [Battles of Lexington and Concord](/wiki/Battles_of_Lexington_and_Concord "Battles of Lexington and Concord").[[67]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016132-72) Washington hastily departed Mount Vernon on May 4 to join the [Second Continental Congress](/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress "Second Continental Congress") in [Philadelphia](/wiki/Philadelphia "Philadelphia").[[68]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010182-73) On June 14, Congress created the [Continental Army](/wiki/Continental_Army "Continental Army") and [John Adams](/wiki/John_Adams "John Adams") nominated Washington as its [commander-in-chief](/wiki/Commander-in-chief "Commander-in-chief"), mainly because of his military experience and the belief that a Virginian would better unite the colonies. He was unanimously elected by Congress the next day.[[f]](#cite_note-75)[[70]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016132–133Ellis200467–68Chernow2010185–186Cogliano202494–95-76) Washington gave an acceptance speech on June 16, declining a salary, though he was later reimbursed expenses.[[71]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERasmussenTilton1999294Rhodehamel20175:_)

Congress chose Washington's primary staff officers, including [Artemas Ward](/wiki/Artemas_Ward "Artemas Ward"), [Horatio Gates](/wiki/Horatio_Gates "Horatio Gates"), [Charles Lee](/wiki/Charles_Lee_(general) "Charles Lee (general)"), [Philip Schuyler](/wiki/Philip_Schuyler "Philip Schuyler"), and [Nathanael Greene](/wiki/Nathanael_Greene "Nathanael Greene").[[72]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010190–191Ferling2002108-78) [Henry Knox](/wiki/Henry_Knox "Henry Knox") impressed Adams and Washington with his knowledge of [ordnance](/wiki/Materiel#Military "Materiel") and was promoted to colonel and chief of artillery. Similarly, Washington was impressed by [Alexander Hamilton](/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton "Alexander Hamilton")'s intelligence and bravery; he would later promote Hamilton to colonel and appoint him his [aide-de-camp](/wiki/Aide-de-camp "Aide-de-camp").[[73]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002109–110Puls200831-79)

Washington initially banned the enlistment of Black soldiers, both free and enslaved. The British saw an opportunity to divide the colonies: the colonial governor of Virginia issued [a proclamation](/wiki/Dunmore%27s_Proclamation "Dunmore's Proclamation") promising freedom to slaves if they joined the British forces.[[74]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000290–291-80) In response to this proclamation and the need for troops, Washington soon overturned his ban.[[75]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPainter200665Hirschfeld1997-81) By the end of the war, around one-tenth of the soldiers in the Continental Army were Black, with some obtaining freedom.[[76]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016231-82)

### Siege of Boston

Main article: [Siege of Boston](/wiki/Siege_of_Boston "Siege of Boston")

In April 1775, in response to the growing rebellious movement, British troops occupied [Boston](/wiki/Boston "Boston"), led by General [Thomas Gage](/wiki/Thomas_Gage "Thomas Gage"), commander of British forces in America.[[77]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016121–123-83) Local militias surrounded the city and trapped the British troops, resulting in a standoff.[[78]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016121–122,_143-84) As Washington headed for Boston, he was greeted by cheering crowds and political ceremony; he became a symbol of the [Patriot](/wiki/Patriot_(American_Revolution) "Patriot (American Revolution)") cause.[[79]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010193-85) Upon Washington's arrival on July 2, he went to inspect the army, but found undisciplined militia.[[80]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016143-86) After consultation, he initiated [Benjamin Franklin](/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin "Benjamin Franklin")'s suggested reforms, instituting military drills and imposing strict disciplinary measures.[[81]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIsaacson2003303Ferling2002112Taylor2016143Fitzpatrick1936514-87) Washington promoted some of the soldiers who had performed well at [Bunker Hill](/wiki/Battle_of_Bunker_Hill "Battle of Bunker Hill") to officer rank, and removed officers who he saw as incompetent.[[82]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002112–113,_116-88) In October, King [George III](/wiki/George_III "George III") declared that the colonies were in open rebellion and relieved Gage of command, replacing him with General [William Howe](/wiki/William_Howe,_5th_Viscount_Howe "William Howe, 5th Viscount Howe").[[83]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010208Taylor2016133–135-89)

When the [Charles River](/wiki/Charles_River "Charles River") froze over, Washington was eager to cross and storm Boston, but Gates and others were opposed to having untrained militia attempt to assault well-garrisoned fortifications. Instead, Washington agreed to [secure the Dorchester Heights](/wiki/Fortification_of_Dorchester_Heights "Fortification of Dorchester Heights") above Boston to try to force the British out.[[84]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009100-90) On March 17, 8,906 British troops, 1,100 [Loyalists](/wiki/Loyalist_(American_Revolution) "Loyalist (American Revolution)"), and 1,220 women and children began a chaotic naval evacuation. Washington entered the city with 500 men, giving them explicit orders not to plunder.[[85]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2005105–107-91) He refrained from exerting military authority in Boston, leaving civilian matters in the hands of local authorities.[[g]](#cite_note-94)[[88]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010227–228Lengel2005124–126Ferling2002116–119Taylor2016144,_153–154-95)

### New York and New Jersey

Main article: [New York and New Jersey Campaign](/wiki/New_York_and_New_Jersey_Campaign "New York and New Jersey Campaign")

#### Battle of Long Island

Main article: [Battle of Long Island](/wiki/Battle_of_Long_Island "Battle of Long Island")

After the victory at Boston, Washington correctly guessed that the British would return to New York City and retaliate. He arrived there on April 13, 1776, and ordered the construction of fortifications. He also ordered his forces to treat civilians and their property with respect, to avoid the abuses Bostonians suffered at the hands of British troops.[[89]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010229–230-96) The British forces, including more than a hundred ships and thousands of troops, began arriving on [Staten Island](/wiki/Staten_Island "Staten Island") in July to lay siege to the city.[[90]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer200432–33Taylor2016162–163-97)

Howe's troop strength totaled 32,000 regulars and [Hessian auxiliaries](/wiki/Hessian_(soldier) "Hessian (soldier)"); Washington had 23,000 men, mostly untrained recruits and militia.[[91]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010244–245Taylor2016162–163-98) In August, Howe landed 20,000 troops at [Gravesend, Brooklyn](/wiki/Gravesend,_Brooklyn "Gravesend, Brooklyn"), and approached Washington's fortifications. Overruling his generals, Washington chose to fight, based on inaccurate information that Howe's army had only around 8,000 soldiers.[[92]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis200495–96Chernow2010244-99) In the [Battle of Long Island](/wiki/Battle_of_Long_Island "Battle of Long Island"), Howe assaulted Washington's flank and inflicted 1,500 Patriot casualties.[[93]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016164-100) Washington retreated to [Manhattan](/wiki/Manhattan "Manhattan").[[94]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2005186–195-101)

Howe sent a message to Washington to negotiate peace, addressing him as "George Washington, Esq." Washington declined to accept the message, demanding to be addressed with diplomatic protocol—not as a rebel.[[95]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010240Pearson2009157–158Taylor2016164-102) Despite misgivings, Washington heeded the advice of General Greene to defend [Fort Washington](/wiki/Fort_Washington_(Manhattan) "Fort Washington (Manhattan)"), but was ultimately forced to abandon it.[[96]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2005236–237Chernow2010257–262Rhodehamel20176:_Winter_Soldier-103) Howe pursued and Washington retreated across the [Hudson River](/wiki/Hudson_River "Hudson River") to [Fort Lee](/wiki/Fort_Lee_Historic_Park "Fort Lee Historic Park"). In November, Howe [captured Fort Washington](/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Washington "Battle of Fort Washington"). Loyalists in New York City considered Howe a liberator and spread a rumor that Washington had set fire to the city.[[97]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996137Taylor2016165-104) Now reduced to 5,400 troops, Washington's army retreated through [New Jersey](/wiki/Province_of_New_Jersey "Province of New Jersey").[[98]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004224–226Taylor2016166–169-105)

#### Crossing the Delaware, Trenton, and Princeton

Main articles: [George Washington's crossing of the Delaware River](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_crossing_of_the_Delaware_River "George Washington's crossing of the Delaware River"), [Battle of Trenton](/wiki/Battle_of_Trenton "Battle of Trenton"), and [Battle of Princeton](/wiki/Battle_of_Princeton "Battle of Princeton")

Washington crossed the [Delaware River](/wiki/Delaware_River "Delaware River") into [Pennsylvania](/wiki/Pennsylvania "Pennsylvania"), where General [John Sullivan](/wiki/John_Sullivan_(general) "John Sullivan (general)") joined him with 2,000 more troops.[[99]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016166–167,_169-106) The future of the Continental Army was in doubt due to a lack of supplies, a harsh winter, expiring enlistments, and [desertions](/wiki/Desertion "Desertion").[[100]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKetchum1999235Chernow2010264-107) Howe posted a Hessian garrison at [Trenton](/wiki/Trenton,_New_Jersey "Trenton, New Jersey") to hold western New Jersey and the east shore of the Delaware.[[101]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016169-108) At sunrise on December 26, 1776, Washington, aided by Colonel Knox and artillery, led his men in [a successful surprise attack on the Hessians](/wiki/Battle_of_Trenton "Battle of Trenton").[[102]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010270,_275–276Ferling2002146–147Fischer2004170,_232–234,_254,_405-109)

Washington returned to New Jersey on January 3, 1777, launching [an attack](/wiki/Battle_of_Princeton "Battle of Princeton") on the British regulars at [Princeton](/wiki/Princeton,_New_Jersey "Princeton, New Jersey"), with 40 Americans killed or wounded and 273 British killed or captured.[[103]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004254Ketchum1999306–307Alden1996146-110) Howe retreated to New York City for the winter.[[104]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016172-111) Washington took up winter headquarters in [Morristown, New Jersey](/wiki/Morristown,_New_Jersey "Morristown, New Jersey").[[105]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatterson2004101-112) Strategically, Washington's victories at Trenton and Princeton were pivotal: they revived Patriot morale and quashed the British strategy of showing overwhelming force followed by offering generous terms, changing the course of the war.[[106]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016172Fischer2004367WillcoxArnstein1988164-113)

### Philadelphia

Main article: [Philadelphia campaign](/wiki/Philadelphia_campaign "Philadelphia campaign")

#### Brandywine, Germantown, and Saratoga

Main articles: [Battle of Brandywine](/wiki/Battle_of_Brandywine "Battle of Brandywine"), [Battle of Germantown](/wiki/Battle_of_Germantown "Battle of Germantown"), and [Battles of Saratoga](/wiki/Battles_of_Saratoga "Battles of Saratoga")

In July 1777, the British general [John Burgoyne](/wiki/John_Burgoyne "John Burgoyne") led his British troops south from [Quebec](/wiki/Quebec "Quebec") in the [Saratoga campaign](/wiki/Saratoga_campaign "Saratoga campaign"); he [recaptured Fort Ticonderoga](/wiki/Siege_of_Fort_Ticonderoga_(1777) "Siege of Fort Ticonderoga (1777)"), intending to divide [New England](/wiki/New_England "New England"). However, General Howe took his army from New York City south to Philadelphia rather than joining Burgoyne near [Albany](/wiki/Albany,_New_York "Albany, New York").[[107]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010300–301-114) Washington and [Gilbert, Marquis de Lafayette](/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_Marquis_de_Lafayette "Gilbert du Motier, Marquis de Lafayette") rushed to Philadelphia to engage Howe. In the [Battle of Brandywine](/wiki/Battle_of_Brandywine "Battle of Brandywine") on September 11, 1777, Howe outmaneuvered Washington and marched unopposed into the American capital at Philadelphia. A Patriot [attack](/wiki/Battle_of_Germantown "Battle of Germantown") against the British at [Germantown](/wiki/Germantown,_Philadelphia "Germantown, Philadelphia") in October failed.[[108]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997340–341Chernow2010301–304-115)

In [Upstate New York](/wiki/Upstate_New_York "Upstate New York"), the Patriots were led by General Horatio Gates. Concerned about Burgoyne's movements southward, Washington sent reinforcements north with Generals [Benedict Arnold](/wiki/Benedict_Arnold "Benedict Arnold") and [Benjamin Lincoln](/wiki/Benjamin_Lincoln "Benjamin Lincoln"). On October 7, 1777, Burgoyne tried to take [Bemis Heights](/wiki/Battles_of_Saratoga "Battles of Saratoga") but was isolated from support and forced to surrender. Gates' victory emboldened Washington's critics, who favored Gates as a military leader.[[109]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010312–313-116) According to the biographer John Alden, "It was inevitable that the defeats of Washington's forces and the concurrent victory of the forces in upper New York should be compared."[[110]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996163-117) Admiration for Washington was waning.[[111]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELenderStone201636–37-118)

#### Valley Forge and Monmouth

Main articles: [Valley Forge](/wiki/Valley_Forge "Valley Forge") and [Battle of Monmouth](/wiki/Battle_of_Monmouth "Battle of Monmouth")

Washington and his army of 11,000 men went into winter quarters at [Valley Forge](/wiki/Valley_Forge "Valley Forge") north of Philadelphia in December 1777. There they lost between 2,000 and 3,000 men as a result of disease and lack of food, clothing, and shelter, reducing the army to below 9,000 men.[[112]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2002186Alden1996165,_167-119) By February, Washington was facing low troop morale and increased desertions.[[113]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996165-120) An [internal revolt](/wiki/Conway_Cabal "Conway Cabal") by his officers prompted some members of Congress to consider removing Washington from command. Washington's supporters resisted, and the matter was ultimately dropped.[[114]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeydt2005-121)

Washington made repeated petitions to Congress for provisions and expressed the urgency of the situation to a congressional delegation.[[115]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart2021242–244-122) Congress agreed to strengthen the army's supply lines and reorganize the [quartermaster](/wiki/Quartermaster "Quartermaster") and [commissary](/wiki/Commissary#Military "Commissary") departments, while Washington launched the [Grand Forage of 1778](/w/index.php?title=Grand_Forage_of_1778&action=edit&redlink=1 "Grand Forage of 1778 (page does not exist)")[[h]](#cite_note-123) to collect food from the surrounding region.[[116]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarp201744–47Herrera20222Bodle200436–40,_215–216-124) Meanwhile, Baron [Friedrich Wilhelm von Steuben](/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_von_Steuben "Friedrich Wilhelm von Steuben")'s incessant drilling transformed Washington's recruits into a disciplined fighting force.[[117]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997342,_356,_359Ferling2009172Alden1996168-125) Washington appointed him [Inspector General](/wiki/Office_of_the_Inspector_General_of_the_United_States_Army "Office of the Inspector General of the United States Army").[[118]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELengel2005281-126)

In early 1778, the French entered into a [Treaty of Alliance](/wiki/Treaty_of_Alliance_(1778) "Treaty of Alliance (1778)") with the Americans.[[119]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016188-127) In May, Howe resigned and was replaced by [Sir Henry Clinton](/wiki/Henry_Clinton_(British_Army_officer,_born_1730) "Henry Clinton (British Army officer, born 1730)").[[120]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2007296-128) The British evacuated Philadelphia for New York that June and Washington summoned a war council of American and French generals. He chose to order a limited strike on the retreating British. Generals Lee and Lafayette moved with 4,000 men, without Washington's knowledge, and bungled their first strike on June 28. Washington relieved Lee and achieved a draw after [an expansive battle](/wiki/Battle_of_Monmouth "Battle of Monmouth"). The British continued their retreat to New York.[[121]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996176–177Ferling2002195–198-129) This battle "marked the end of the war's campaigning in the northern and middle states. Washington would not fight the British in a major engagement again for more than three years".[[122]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhodehamel20177:_Brandywine,_Germantown,_and_Monmouth-130) British attention shifted to the Southern theatre; in late 1778, General Clinton captured [Savannah, Georgia](/wiki/Savannah,_Georgia "Savannah, Georgia"), a key port in the American South.[[123]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016230-131) Washington, meanwhile, ordered [an expedition](/wiki/Sullivan_Expedition "Sullivan Expedition") against the [Iroquois](/wiki/Iroquois "Iroquois"), the Indigenous allies of the British, destroying their villages.[[124]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhodehamel20178:_The_Great_Man-132)

### Espionage and West Point

Main articles: [Culper Ring](/wiki/Culper_Ring "Culper Ring") and [Military career of Benedict Arnold, 1777–1779](/wiki/Military_career_of_Benedict_Arnold,_1777%E2%80%931779 "Military career of Benedict Arnold, 1777–1779")

Washington became America's first [spymaster](/wiki/Spymaster "Spymaster") by designing an espionage system against the British.[[125]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTENagy2016274-133) In 1778, Major [Benjamin Tallmadge](/wiki/Benjamin_Tallmadge "Benjamin Tallmadge") formed the [Culper Ring](/wiki/Culper_Ring "Culper Ring") at Washington's direction to covertly collect information about the British in New York.[[126]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose200675,_224,_258–261-134) Intelligence from the Culper Ring saved French forces from a surprise British attack, which was itself based on intelligence from Washington's general turned British spy Benedict Arnold.[[127]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose2006[httpsarchiveorgdetailswashingtonsspies00alexpagen207mode2up_n207]-135)

Washington had disregarded incidents of disloyalty by Arnold, who had distinguished himself in many campaigns, including [the invasion of Quebec](/wiki/Benedict_Arnold%27s_expedition_to_Quebec "Benedict Arnold's expedition to Quebec").[[128]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010378–387Philbrick201635-136) In 1779, Arnold began supplying the British spymaster [John André](/wiki/John_Andr%C3%A9 "John André") with sensitive information intended to allow the British to capture [West Point](/wiki/West_Point,_New_York "West Point, New York"), a key American defensive position on the Hudson River.[[129]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhilbrick2016250–251,_269-137) On September 21, Arnold gave André plans to take over the garrison.[[130]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer2006-138) André was captured by militia who discovered the plans, after which Arnold escaped to New York.[[131]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhodehamel20178:_The_Great_ManPalmer2006410-139) On being told about Arnold's treason, Washington recalled the commanders positioned under Arnold at key points around the fort to prevent any complicity. He assumed personal command at West Point and reorganized its defenses.[[132]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer2006370–371Middlekauff2015232-140)

### Southern theater and Yorktown

Main articles: [Southern theater of the American Revolutionary War](/wiki/Southern_theater_of_the_American_Revolutionary_War "Southern theater of the American Revolutionary War") and [Siege of Yorktown](/wiki/Siege_of_Yorktown "Siege of Yorktown")

By June 1780, the British had occupied the South Carolina [Piedmont](/wiki/Piedmont_(United_States) "Piedmont (United States)") and had firm control of the South. Washington was reinvigorated, however, when Lafayette returned from France with more ships, men, and supplies,[[133]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016234Alden1996187–188-141) and 5,000 veteran French troops led by Marshal [Rochambeau](/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Donatien_de_Vimeur,_comte_de_Rochambeau "Jean-Baptiste Donatien de Vimeur, comte de Rochambeau") arrived at [Newport, Rhode Island](/wiki/Newport,_Rhode_Island "Newport, Rhode Island") in July.[[134]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELancasterPlumb1985311-142)

General Clinton sent Arnold, now a British brigadier general, to Virginia in December with 1,700 troops to capture [Portsmouth](/wiki/Portsmouth,_Virginia "Portsmouth, Virginia") and conduct raids on Patriot forces. Washington sent Lafayette south to counter Arnold's efforts.[[135]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010403-143) Washington initially hoped to bring the fight to New York, drawing the British forces away from Virginia and ending the war there, but Rochambeau advised him that [Cornwallis](/wiki/Charles_Cornwallis,_1st_Marquess_Cornwallis "Charles Cornwallis, 1st Marquess Cornwallis") in Virginia was the better target.[[136]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996198–199Chernow2010403–404-144) On August 19, 1781, Washington and Rochambeau began a march to [Yorktown, Virginia](/wiki/Yorktown,_Virginia "Yorktown, Virginia"), known now as the "[celebrated march](/wiki/Washington%E2%80%93Rochambeau_Revolutionary_Route "Washington–Rochambeau Revolutionary Route")".[[137]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELengel2005335-145) Washington was in command of an army of 7,800 Frenchmen, 3,100 militia, and 8,000 Continental troops. Inexperienced in siege warfare, he often deferred to the judgment of Rochambeau. Despite this, Rochambeau never challenged Washington's authority as the battle's commanding officer.[[138]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010413-146)

By late September, Patriot-French forces surrounded Yorktown, trapping the British Army, while the French navy emerged victorious at the [Battle of the Chesapeake](/wiki/Battle_of_the_Chesapeake "Battle of the Chesapeake"). The final American offensive began with a shot fired by Washington.[[124]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhodehamel20178:_The_Great_Man-132) The siege ended with a British surrender on October 19, 1781; over 7,000 British soldiers became [prisoners of war](/wiki/Prisoner_of_war "Prisoner of war").[[139]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996198,_201Chernow2010372–373,_418Lengel2005337-147) Washington negotiated the terms of surrender for two days, and the official signing ceremony took place on October 19.[[140]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMann200838LancasterPlumb1985254Chernow2010419-148) Although the peace treaty was not negotiated for two more years, Yorktown proved to be the last significant battle of the Revolutionary War, with the British Parliament agreeing to cease hostilities in March 1782.[[141]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFleming2007194,_312-149)

### Demobilization and resignation

Main article: [George Washington's resignation as commander-in-chief](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_resignation_as_commander-in-chief "George Washington's resignation as commander-in-chief")

When peace negotiations began in April 1782, both the British and French began gradually evacuating their forces.[[142]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016313–315-150) In March 1783, Washington successfully calmed the [Newburgh Conspiracy](/wiki/Newburgh_Conspiracy "Newburgh Conspiracy"), a planned mutiny by American officers dissatisfied with a lack of pay.[[31]](#cite_note-anb-34)[[143]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrowne201611–16-151) Washington submitted an account of $450,000 in expenses which he had advanced to the army. The account was settled, though it was vague about large sums and included expenses his wife had incurred through visits to his headquarters.[[144]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996209-152)

When the [Treaty of Paris](/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783) "Treaty of Paris (1783)") was signed on September 3, 1783, Britain officially recognized American independence. Washington disbanded his army, giving a farewell address to his soldiers on November 2.[[145]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELengel2005350-153) He oversaw the [evacuation of British forces in New York](/wiki/Evacuation_Day_(New_York) "Evacuation Day (New York)") and was greeted by parades and celebrations.[[146]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010446,_448–449,_451Puls2008184–186-154)

In early December 1783, Washington bade farewell to his officers at [Fraunces Tavern](/wiki/Fraunces_Tavern "Fraunces Tavern") and [resigned as commander-in-chief](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_resignation_as_commander-in-chief "George Washington's resignation as commander-in-chief") soon after.[[147]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016319-155) In a final appearance in uniform, he gave a statement to the Congress: "I consider it an indispensable duty to close this last solemn act of my official life, by commending the interests of our dearest country to the protection of Almighty God, and those who have the superintendence of them, to His holy keeping."[[148]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996210Chernow2010451–452,_455-156) Washington's resignation was acclaimed at home and abroad, "extolled by later historians as a signal event that set the country's political course" according to the historian [Edward J. Larson](/wiki/Edward_J._Larson "Edward J. Larson").[[149]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELarson201410Wood1992206-157)[[i]](#cite_note-159) The same month, Washington was appointed president-general of the [Society of the Cincinnati](/wiki/Society_of_the_Cincinnati "Society of the Cincinnati"), a newly established hereditary fraternity of Revolutionary War officers.[[151]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010444-160)

## Early republic (1783–1789)

Further information: [Confederation Period](/wiki/Confederation_Period "Confederation Period") and [Articles of Confederation](/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation "Articles of Confederation")

### Return to Mount Vernon

> "I am not only retired from all public employments but I am retiring within myself, and shall be able to view the solitary walk and tread the paths of private life with heartfelt satisfaction ... I will move gently down the stream of life, until I sleep with my fathers."

— George Washington in a letter to Lafayette. February 1, 1784.[[152]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997410Flexner1974182–183DalzellDalzell1998112-161)

After spending just ten days at Mount Vernon out of 8+1⁄2 years of war, Washington was eager to return home. He arrived on Christmas Eve; Professor [John E. Ferling](/wiki/John_E._Ferling "John E. Ferling") wrote that he was delighted to be "free of the bustle of a camp and the busy scenes of public life".[[153]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009246-162) He received a constant stream of visitors paying their respects at Mount Vernon.[[154]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010462Ferling2009255–256-163)

Washington reactivated his interests in the [Great Dismal Swamp](/wiki/Great_Dismal_Swamp "Great Dismal Swamp") and [Potomac Canal](/wiki/Potomac_Company "Potomac Company") projects, begun before the war, though neither paid him any dividends.[[155]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2010332–334-164) He undertook a 34-day, 680-mile (1,090 km) trip in 1784 to check on his land holdings in the Ohio Country.[[156]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009247–255-165) He oversaw the completion of remodeling work at Mount Vernon, which transformed his residence into the mansion that survives to this day—although his financial situation was not strong. Creditors paid him in [depreciated](/wiki/Depreciation "Depreciation") wartime currency, and he owed significant amounts in taxes and wages. Mount Vernon had made no profit during his absence, and he saw persistently poor crop yields due to pestilence and bad weather. His estate recorded its eleventh year running at a deficit in 1787.[[157]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009246–247Chernow2010552–553Ellis2004167-166)

To make his estate profitable again, Washington undertook a new landscaping plan and succeeded in cultivating a range of fast-growing trees and native shrubs.[[158]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWulf201152Subak201843–44-167) He also began breeding [mules](/wiki/Mule "Mule") after being gifted a [stud](/wiki/Stud_(animal) "Stud (animal)") by King [Charles III of Spain](/wiki/Charles_III_of_Spain "Charles III of Spain") in 1785;[[159]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoe2020xxii-168) he believed that they would revolutionize agriculture.[[160]](#cite_note-169)

### Constitutional Convention of 1787

Main article: [Constitutional Convention (United States)](/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States) "Constitutional Convention (United States)")

Before returning to private life in June 1783, Washington called for a strong union. Though he was concerned that he might be criticized for meddling in civil matters, he sent a circular letter to the states, maintaining that the [Articles of Confederation](/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation "Articles of Confederation") were no more than "a rope of sand". He believed the nation was on the verge of "anarchy and confusion", was vulnerable to foreign intervention, and that a national constitution would unify the states under a strong central government.[[161]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996221Chernow2010518Ferling2009266-170)

When [Shays's Rebellion](/wiki/Shays%27s_Rebellion "Shays's Rebellion") erupted in Massachusetts in August 1786, Washington was further convinced that a national constitution was needed.[[162]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010517–519-171)[[31]](#cite_note-anb-34) Some nationalists feared that the new republic had descended into lawlessness, and they met on September 11, 1786, at [Annapolis](/wiki/Annapolis_Convention_(1786) "Annapolis Convention (1786)") to ask the Congress to revise the Articles of Confederation.[[163]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2016373–374Ferling2009266-172) Congress agreed to a [Constitutional Convention](/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States) "Constitutional Convention (United States)") to be held in Philadelphia in 1787, with each state to send delegates.[[164]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010523Taylor2016373–374-173) Washington was chosen to lead the Virginia delegation, but he declined. He had concerns about the legality of the convention and consulted [James Madison](/wiki/James_Madison "James Madison"), Henry Knox, and others. They persuaded him to attend as they felt his presence might induce reluctant states to send delegates and smooth the way for the ratification process while also giving legitimacy to the convention.[[165]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010220–221Ferling2009266-174)

Washington arrived in Philadelphia on May 9, 1787, and the convention began on May 25. Benjamin Franklin nominated Washington to preside over the meeting, and he was unanimously elected.[[166]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010520–521,_523,_526,_529Unger201333-175) The delegate [Edmund Randolph](/wiki/Edmund_Randolph "Edmund Randolph") introduced Madison's [Virginia Plan](/wiki/Virginia_Plan "Virginia Plan"); it called for an entirely new constitution and a sovereign national government, which Washington highly recommended.[[167]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2010359–360-176) However, details around representation were particularly contentious, resulting in a competing [New Jersey Plan](/wiki/New_Jersey_Plan "New Jersey Plan") being brought forward.[[168]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010553-177) On July 10, Washington wrote to Alexander Hamilton: "I almost despair of seeing a favorable issue to the proceedings of our convention and do therefore repent having had any agency in the business."[[169]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996226–227-178) Nevertheless, he lent his prestige to the work of the other delegates, lobbying many to support the ratification of the [Constitution](/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States "Constitution of the United States").[[170]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996229-179) The final version adopted the [Connecticut Compromise](/wiki/Connecticut_Compromise "Connecticut Compromise") between the two plans, and was [signed by 39 of 55 delegates](/wiki/Signing_of_the_United_States_Constitution "Signing of the United States Constitution") on September 17, 1787.[[171]](#cite_note-180)

### First presidential election

Main article: [1788–1789 United States presidential election](/wiki/1788%E2%80%931789_United_States_presidential_election "1788–1789 United States presidential election")

Just prior to the first presidential election of 1789, in 1788 Washington was appointed [chancellor of the College of William & Mary](/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_College_of_William_%26_Mary "Chancellor of the College of William & Mary").[[172]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorrison20096-181) He continued to serve through his presidency until his death.[[173]](#cite_note-chancellor-182) The delegates to the convention for the first presidential election anticipated a Washington presidency and left it to him to define the office once elected.[[169]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlden1996226–227-178) When the state electors voted on February 4, 1789,[[174]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010559–560Ferling2009361-183) Washington was unanimously elected, unique among U.S. presidents.[[175]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010551-184) John Adams was elected vice president.[[176]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009274-185) Despite writing that he felt "anxious and painful sensations" about leaving Mount Vernon, Washington departed for New York City on April 16.[[177]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009274–275Chernow2010559–561Rhodehamel201710:_)

## Presidency (1789–1797)

Main article: [Presidency of George Washington](/wiki/Presidency_of_George_Washington "Presidency of George Washington")

| Washington cabinet |

| [President](/wiki/President_of_the_United_States "President of the United States") | George Washington | 1789–1797 |
| [Vice President](/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States "Vice President of the United States") | [John Adams](/wiki/John_Adams "John Adams") | 1789–1797 |
| [Secretary of State](/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State "United States Secretary of State") | [John Jay](/wiki/John_Jay "John Jay") (acting) | 1789–1790 |
| [Thomas Jefferson](/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson "Thomas Jefferson") | 1790–1793 |
| [Edmund Randolph](/wiki/Edmund_Randolph "Edmund Randolph") | 1794–1795 |
| [Timothy Pickering](/wiki/Timothy_Pickering "Timothy Pickering") | 1795–1797 |
| [Secretary of the Treasury](/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury "United States Secretary of the Treasury") | [Alexander Hamilton](/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton "Alexander Hamilton") | 1789–1795 |

| [Secretary of War](/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War "United States Secretary of War") | [Henry Knox](/wiki/Henry_Knox "Henry Knox") | 1789–1794 |
| [Timothy Pickering](/wiki/Timothy_Pickering "Timothy Pickering") | 1795 |
| [James McHenry](/wiki/James_McHenry "James McHenry") | 1796–1797 |
