| [William Bradford](/wiki/William_Bradford_(Attorney_General) "William Bradford (Attorney General)") | 1794–1795 |
| [Charles Lee](/wiki/Charles_Lee_(Attorney_General) "Charles Lee (Attorney General)") | 1795–1797 |

### First term

Further information: [History of Washington, D.C.](/wiki/History_of_Washington,_D.C. "History of Washington, D.C."); [American School (economics)](/wiki/American_School_(economics) "American School (economics)"); [Whiskey Rebellion](/wiki/Whiskey_Rebellion "Whiskey Rebellion"); [Northwest Indian War](/wiki/Northwest_Indian_War "Northwest Indian War"); [Treaty of New York (1790)](/wiki/Treaty_of_New_York_(1790) "Treaty of New York (1790)"); and [Cabinet of the United States](/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_States "Cabinet of the United States")

Washington was [inaugurated](/wiki/First_inauguration_of_George_Washington "First inauguration of George Washington") on April 30, 1789, taking the [oath of office](/wiki/Oath_of_office_of_the_President_of_the_United_States "Oath of office of the President of the United States") at [Federal Hall](/wiki/Federal_Hall "Federal Hall") in New York City.[[j]](#cite_note-188)[[179]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20024Chernow2010550–551Rhodehamel201710:_) His coach was led by militia and a marching band and followed by statesmen and foreign dignitaries in an inaugural parade, with a crowd of 10,000.[[180]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010566–567Randall1997448-190) [Robert R. Livingston](/wiki/Robert_R._Livingston_(chancellor) "Robert R. Livingston (chancellor)") administered the oath, using a [Bible provided by the Masons](/wiki/George_Washington_Inaugural_Bible "George Washington Inaugural Bible").[[181]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20024Chernow2010568-191) Washington read a speech in the Senate Chamber, asking "that Almighty Being ... consecrate the liberties and happiness of the people of the United States".[[182]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997448Alden1996236-192) Though he wished to serve without a salary, Congress insisted that he receive one,[[31]](#cite_note-anb-34) providing Washington $25,000 annually (compared to $5,000 annually for the vice president).[[183]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010552Rhodehamel201710:_)

Washington wrote to James Madison: "As the first of everything in our situation will serve to establish a precedent, it is devoutly wished on my part that these precedents be fixed on true principles."[[184]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnger201376-194) To that end, he argued against the majestic titles proposed by the Senate, including "His Majesty" and "His Highness the President", in favor of "Mr. President".[[185]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartoloni-Tuazon20141,_9Unger201380-195) His executive precedents included the inaugural address, messages to Congress, and the [cabinet form](/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_States "Cabinet of the United States") of the [executive branch](/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States "Federal government of the United States").[[186]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnger2013236–237-196) He also selected the first justices for the [Supreme Court](/wiki/US_Supreme_Court "US Supreme Court").[[187]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBanner202413-197)

Washington was an able administrator and judge of talent and character.[[188]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004197–198Unger2013236–237-198) The old [Confederation](/wiki/Confederation_Period "Confederation Period") lacked the powers to handle its workload and had weak leadership, no executive, a small bureaucracy of clerks, large debt, worthless paper money, and no power to establish taxes.[[189]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20025-199) Congress created executive departments in 1789, including the [State Department](/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State "United States Department of State"), the [War Department](/wiki/United_States_Department_of_War "United States Department of War"), and the [Treasury Department](/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Treasury "United States Department of the Treasury"). Washington appointed Edmund Randolph as [Attorney General](/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General "United States Attorney General"), [Samuel Osgood](/wiki/Samuel_Osgood "Samuel Osgood") as [Postmaster General](/wiki/Postmaster_General "Postmaster General"), [Thomas Jefferson](/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson "Thomas Jefferson") as [Secretary of State](/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State "United States Secretary of State"), Henry Knox as [Secretary of War](/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War "United States Secretary of War"), and Alexander Hamilton as [Secretary of the Treasury](/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury "United States Secretary of the Treasury"). Washington's cabinet became a consulting and advisory body, not mandated by the Constitution.[[190]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009281–282Cooke20024–5Chervinsky20204–5-200) Washington restricted cabinet discussions to topics of his choosing and expected department heads to agreeably carry out his decisions.[[189]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20025-199) He exercised restraint in using [his veto power](/wiki/List_of_United_States_presidential_vetoes "List of United States presidential vetoes"), writing that "I give my Signature to many Bills with which my Judgment is at variance."[[191]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis1999133-201)

Washington opposed political factionalism and remained non-partisan throughout his presidency (the only United States president to do so). He was sympathetic to a Federalist form of government.[[192]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010696–698Randall1997478-202) Washington's closest advisors formed two factions, portending the [First Party System](/wiki/First_Party_System "First Party System"). Hamilton formed the [Federalist Party](/wiki/Federalist_Party "Federalist Party") to promote national credit and a financially powerful nation. Jefferson opposed Hamilton's agenda and founded the [Jeffersonian Republicans](/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party "Democratic-Republican Party"). Washington favored Hamilton's agenda, however, and it ultimately went into effect—resulting in bitter controversy.[[193]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke20027-203) Other domestic issues during Washington's first term included the planning of a permanent capital,[[194]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBordewich2016150–157-204) the passage of several constitutional amendments including the [Bill of Rights](/wiki/United_States_Bill_of_Rights "United States Bill of Rights"), and continuing debates concerning slavery[[195]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBordewich2016198–206,_213–220-205) and expansion into Native American territory.[[196]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGenoveseLandry202134–38-206) Washington proclaimed November 26, 1789, as a day of [Thanksgiving](/wiki/Thanksgiving_(United_States) "Thanksgiving (United States)") to encourage national unity.[[197]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010585,_609Henriques200665NovakNovak2007144–146-207)

### Second term

Washington initially planned to retire after his first term, weary of office and in poor health. After dealing with the infighting in his cabinet and with partisan critics, he showed little enthusiasm for a second term, and Martha wanted him not to run.[[198]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010674–675,_678Ferling2009362Randall1997484-208) Washington's nephew George Augustine Washington, managing Mount Vernon in his absence, was critically ill, further increasing Washington's desire to retire.[[199]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010687-209) Many, however, urged him to run for a second term. Madison told him that his absence would allow the dangerous political rift in his cabinet and the House to worsen. Jefferson also pleaded with him not to retire, pledging to drop his attacks on Hamilton.[[200]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2010421Randall1997482Chernow2010675,_678-210) Hamilton maintained that Washington's absence would be "deplored as the greatest evil" to the country.[[201]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2005403-211) With the [election of 1792](/wiki/1792_United_States_presidential_election "1792 United States presidential election") nearing, Washington agreed to run.[[202]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200210-212) On February 13, 1793, the [Electoral College](/wiki/United_States_Electoral_College "United States Electoral College") unanimously re-elected Washington president, while John Adams was re-elected as vice president by a vote of 77 to 50.[[202]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200210-212) Washington was sworn into office by Associate Justice [William Cushing](/wiki/William_Cushing "William Cushing") on March 4, 1793, in [Congress Hall](/wiki/Congress_Hall "Congress Hall") in Philadelphia.[[203]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010687Cooke200210–11-213)

On April 22, 1793, after the [French Revolutionary Wars](/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars "French Revolutionary Wars") broke out, Washington [issued a proclamation](/wiki/Proclamation_of_Neutrality "Proclamation of Neutrality") declaring American neutrality. He was resolved to pursue "a conduct friendly and impartial toward the belligerent Powers" while warning Americans not to intervene in the conflict.[[204]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009299,_304,_308–311Banning19742Cooke200211–12-214) Although Washington recognized France's revolutionary government, he eventually asked that the French minister to the United States, [Edmond-Charles Genêt](/wiki/Edmond-Charles_Gen%C3%AAt "Edmond-Charles Genêt"), be recalled.[[205]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200212–13-215) Genêt was a diplomatic troublemaker who was openly hostile toward Washington's neutrality policy. He procured four American ships as privateers to strike at Spanish forces (British allies) in [Florida](/wiki/Florida "Florida") while organizing militias to strike at other British possessions. However, his efforts failed to draw the United States into the conflict.[[206]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010692Cooke200212-216)

During his second term Washington faced two major domestic conflicts. The first was the [Whiskey Rebellion](/wiki/Whiskey_Rebellion "Whiskey Rebellion") (1791–1794), a Pennsylvania revolt against liquor taxation. Washington mobilized a militia and personally commanded an expedition against the rebels which suppressed the insurgency.[[207]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004225-217)[[31]](#cite_note-anb-34) The second was the [Northwest Indian War](/wiki/Northwest_Indian_War "Northwest Indian War") between White settlers and Native Americans who were supported by the British; the latter were stationed in forts that they had refused to abandon after the Revolutionary War.[[31]](#cite_note-anb-34)[[208]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenn199317-218) In 1794 American troops defeated the Native American forces at the [Battle of Fallen Timbers](/wiki/Battle_of_Fallen_Timbers "Battle of Fallen Timbers"), ending the conflict between the two.[[31]](#cite_note-anb-34)

Hamilton formulated the [Jay Treaty](/wiki/Jay_Treaty "Jay Treaty") to normalize trade relations with Britain while removing them from western forts, and also to resolve financial debts remaining from the Revolution.[[209]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1995ch._9-219) Chief Justice [John Jay](/wiki/John_Jay "John Jay") represented Washington's position and signed the treaty on November 19, 1794. Washington supported the treaty because it avoided war,[[210]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010730-220) although he was disappointed that its provisions favored Britain.[[211]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009340-221) He mobilized public opinion and secured ratification[[212]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEstes2000409–420Estes2001127-222) but faced frequent public criticism and political controversy.[[213]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009344-223)[[31]](#cite_note-anb-34) Following the British abandonment of their forts around the [Great Lakes](/wiki/Great_Lakes "Great Lakes"), the proposed position of the [Canada–United States border](/wiki/Canada%E2%80%93United_States_border "Canada–United States border") was sent to [arbitration](/wiki/Arbitration "Arbitration"). Numerous pre-Revolution debts were settled and the British opened the [British West Indies](/wiki/British_West_Indies "British West Indies") to American merchants. The agreement secured peace with Britain and a decade of prosperous trade; however, Jefferson claimed that it angered France and "invited rather than avoided" war.[[214]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009343-224) Jefferson's claim was verified when relations with France deteriorated after the signing of the treaty, with the [French Directory](/wiki/French_Directory "French Directory") authorizing the seizure of American ships two days before Washington's term ended.[[215]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAkers200227-225) Succeeding president John Adams was left with the prospect of war.[[216]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrizzard2005263Lengel2005357-226) Relations with the Spanish were more successful: [Thomas Pinckney](/wiki/Thomas_Pinckney "Thomas Pinckney") negotiated the [Treaty of San Lorenzo](/wiki/Treaty_of_San_Lorenzo "Treaty of San Lorenzo") in 1795, settling the border between the United States and Spanish territory, and guaranteeing American navigational access to the [Mississippi River](/wiki/Mississippi_River "Mississippi River")[[31]](#cite_note-anb-34)[[217]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowlan201455-227)

On July 31, 1793, Jefferson submitted his resignation from cabinet.[[218]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke200213-228) Hamilton resigned from office in January 1795 and was replaced by [Oliver Wolcott Jr.](/wiki/Oliver_Wolcott_Jr. "Oliver Wolcott Jr.") Washington's relationship with Secretary of War Henry Knox deteriorated over rumors that Knox had profited from contracts for the construction of U.S. frigates ostensibly commissioned to combat [Barbary pirates](/wiki/Barbary_pirates "Barbary pirates") under the [Naval Act of 1794](/wiki/Naval_Act_of_1794 "Naval Act of 1794"). Knox was forced to resign.[[219]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010713-229)[[220]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010726–727Cooke200215-230) In the final months of his presidency, Washington was assailed by his political foes and a partisan press who accused him of being ambitious and greedy. He came to regard the press as a disuniting force.[[221]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997491–492Chernow2010752–754-231) Washington also opposed demands by Congress to see papers related to the Jay Treaty, arguing that they were not "relative to any purpose under the cognizance of the House of Representatives, except that of an impeachment, which the resolution has not expressed."[[31]](#cite_note-anb-34)

### Farewell Address

Main article: [George Washington's Farewell Address](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_Farewell_Address "George Washington's Farewell Address")

At the end of his second term, Washington retired. He was dismayed with the personal attacks against him and wanted to ensure that a truly contested presidential election could be held. He did not feel bound to a two-term limit, but his retirement set a significant precedent.[[222]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeabody2001440–446-232) In May 1792, in anticipation of his retirement, Washington instructed James Madison to prepare a "[valedictory address](/wiki/Valedictorian "Valedictorian")", an initial draft of which was entitled the "Farewell Address".[[223]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpaldingGarrity199646–47-233) In May 1796, Washington sent the manuscript to Hamilton, who did an extensive rewrite, while Washington provided final edits.[[224]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1972292Chernow2010752–753SpaldingGarrity19964744Hayes2017287–298-234) On September 19, 1796, David Claypoole's *[American Daily Advertiser](/wiki/Pennsylvania_Packet "Pennsylvania Packet")* published the address.[[225]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010754Avlon201789–90-235)

Washington stressed that national identity was paramount, and said that the "name of AMERICAN... must always exalt the just pride of patriotism".[[226]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010755Nowlan201455–56-236) Washington warned against the dangers of political parties and entangling foreign alliances with domestic affairs.[[227]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997492SpaldingGarrity199648,_72-237) He counseled friendship and commerce with all nations, but advised against involvement in European wars.[[228]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFishmanPedersonRozell2001119–120GreggSpalding1999199–216-238) He stressed the importance of religion, asserting that "religion and morality are indispensable supports" in a republic.[[229]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010133-239)

He closed the address by reflecting on his legacy: "I fervently beseech the Almighty to avert or mitigate the evils to which [my unintentioned errors] may tend. I shall also carry with me the hope that my country will never cease to view them with indulgence, and that, after forty-five years of my life dedicated to its service with an upright zeal, the faults of incompetent abilities will be consigned to oblivion, as myself must soon be to the [mansions of rest](/wiki/John_14 "John 14")."[[230]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAvlon2017280-240) After initial publication, many Republicans, including Madison, criticized the address and described it as an anti-French campaign document, with Madison believing that Washington was strongly pro-British.[[231]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpaldingGarrity1996143-241) In 1972, the Washington scholar [James Flexner](/wiki/James_Thomas_Flexner "James Thomas Flexner") referred to the Farewell Address as receiving as much acclaim as Thomas Jefferson's [Declaration of Independence](/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence "United States Declaration of Independence") and [Abraham Lincoln](/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln "Abraham Lincoln")'s [Gettysburg Address](/wiki/Gettysburg_Address "Gettysburg Address").[[232]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1972292SpaldingGarrity1996142-242) In 2010, Chernow called the "Farewell Address" one of the most influential statements on [republicanism](/wiki/Republicanism "Republicanism").[[233]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010752–754-243)

## Post-presidency (1797–1799)

Further information: [Post-presidency of George Washington](/wiki/Post-presidency_of_George_Washington "Post-presidency of George Washington")

### Retirement

See also: [Finances of George Washington](/wiki/Finances_of_George_Washington "Finances of George Washington")

Washington retired to Mount Vernon in March 1797 and devoted time to his business interests.[[234]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERagsdale20215–6-244) His plantation operations were minimally profitable,[[33]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201053-36) and his lands in the west (on the [Piedmont](/wiki/Piedmont_(United_States) "Piedmont (United States)")) yielded little income; he attempted to sell these but was unable to.[[235]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004255–261-245) He became an even more committed Federalist. He vocally supported the [Alien and Sedition Acts](/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts "Alien and Sedition Acts") and convinced Federalist [John Marshall](/wiki/John_Marshall "John Marshall") to run for Congress to weaken the Jeffersonian hold on [Virginia](/wiki/Virginia "Virginia").[[236]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1974386-246) French privateers began seizing American ships in 1798, and deteriorating relations led to the "[Quasi-War](/wiki/Quasi-War "Quasi-War")". Washington wrote to Secretary of War [James McHenry](/wiki/James_McHenry "James McHenry") offering to organize President Adams' army.[[237]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997497-247) Adams nominated him for a lieutenant general commission and the position of commander-in-chief of the armies on July 4, 1798.[[238]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1974376–377Bell199264-248) Washington served as the commanding general from July 13, 1798, until his death 17 months later.[[239]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell199264-249) He participated in planning but delegated the active leadership of the army to Hamilton. No army invaded the United States during this period, and Washington did not assume a field command.[[240]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn1975225–242Grizzard2005264-250)

Washington was known to be rich because of what Chernow calls the "glorified façade of wealth and grandeur" at Mount Vernon.[[241]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010708-251) However, nearly all of his wealth was in the form of land and slaves rather than ready cash. To supplement his income, he erected a [distillery](/wiki/Distillery "Distillery") for [whiskey](/wiki/Whiskey "Whiskey") production.[[242]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirschfeld199744–45Ferling2009351-252) He bought land parcels to spur development around the new [Federal City](/wiki/Washington,_D.C. "Washington, D.C.") (named in his honor in 1791), and he sold individual lots to middle-income investors rather than multiple lots to large investors, believing the former would be more likely to commit to making improvements.[[243]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010663,_704–705-253) At the time of his death in 1799, his estate was worth an estimated $780,000,[[244]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELengel2015246-254) and he held title to more than 58,000 acres (23,000 ha) of land across Virginia, Ohio, Maryland, Pennsylvania, New York, [Kentucky](/wiki/Kentucky "Kentucky"), and the [Northwest Territory](/wiki/Northwest_Territory "Northwest Territory").[[244]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELengel2015246-254) Washington's peak net worth was estimated to be $587 million in 2020 dollars.[[245]](#cite_note-255)

### Death

On December 12, 1799, Washington inspected his farms on horseback in inclement weather for five hours. He then dined with guests without putting on dry clothes.[[246]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806-256) He had a sore throat the next day but was well enough to mark trees for cutting.[[247]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2010505-257) Early the following morning, he awoke to an inflamed throat and difficulty breathing. He ordered his estate overseer, George Rawlins, to remove nearly a pint of his blood ([bloodletting](/wiki/Bloodletting "Bloodletting") was a common practice of the time). His family summoned doctors [James Craik](/wiki/James_Craik "James Craik"), [Gustavus Richard Brown](/wiki/Gustavus_Richard_Brown "Gustavus Richard Brown"), and [Elisha C. Dick](/wiki/Elisha_C._Dick "Elisha C. Dick").[[248]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806–810Morens1999-258) Brown initially believed Washington had [quinsy](/wiki/Peritonsillar_abscess "Peritonsillar abscess"); Dick thought the condition was a more serious "violent inflammation of the membranes of the throat".[[249]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806–807Flexner1974399-259) They continued bloodletting to approximately five pints, but Washington's condition deteriorated further. Dick proposed a [tracheotomy](/wiki/Tracheotomy "Tracheotomy"); the other physicians were not familiar with that procedure and disapproved.[[250]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806–810-260) Washington instructed Brown and Dick to leave the room, while he assured Craik, "Doctor, I die hard, but I am not afraid to go."[[251]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004269-261)

On his deathbed, afraid of being entombed alive, Washington instructed his private secretary [Tobias Lear](/wiki/Tobias_Lear "Tobias Lear") to wait three days before his burial.[[252]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010808-262) According to Lear, Washington died between 10 p.m. and 11 p.m. on December 14, 1799, with Martha seated at the foot of his bed. His last words were "'Tis well."[[253]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlexner1974401–402Chernow2010808–809-263) The diagnosis of Washington's illness and the immediate cause of his death have been subjects of debate. The published account of Craik and Brown stated that his symptoms were consistent with "cynanche trachealis", a term then used to describe severe inflammation of the upper windpipe, including quinsy.[[k]](#cite_note-265) Accusations of [medical malpractice](/wiki/Medical_malpractice "Medical malpractice") have persisted since Washington's death.[[250]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010806–810-260) Modern medical authors have largely concluded that he likely died from severe [epiglottitis](/wiki/Epiglottitis "Epiglottitis") complicated by the treatments he was given, which included multiple doses of [calomel](/wiki/Mercury(I)_chloride "Mercury(I) chloride") (a purgative) and extensive bloodletting, likely resulting in [hypovolemic shock](/wiki/Hypovolemia "Hypovolemia").[[l]](#cite_note-269)

### Funeral and burial

Washington's funeral was held on December 18, 1799, four days after his death, at Mount Vernon. Cavalry and foot soldiers led the procession, and six colonels served as the pallbearers. The Mount Vernon funeral service was restricted mostly to family and friends.[[258]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010808–810-270) Reverend Thomas Davis read a brief funeral service, followed by a ceremony performed by members of Washington's Masonic lodge; Washington had been a Freemason since 1752.[[259]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall199767Chernow201027-271)[[260]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETabbert2022196–197-272) Word of his death traveled slowly, but as it reached other regions, church bells rang and many businesses closed.[[261]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010810–811-273) Memorial processions were held in major cities of the United States. Martha burned her correspondence with Washington to protect its privacy, though five letters between the couple are known to have survived.[[262]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010814-274)

Washington was buried in the Washington family vault at Mount Vernon on December 18, 1799.[[263]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELengel2005vii-275) In his will, Washington left instructions for the construction of a new vault;[[261]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010810–811-273) this was completed in 1831, after a disgruntled ex-employee of the estate [attempted to steal](/wiki/Attempted_theft_of_George_Washington%27s_skull "Attempted theft of George Washington's skull") what he thought was Washington's skull.[[264]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowlan201459Costello202177–78-276) In 1832, a joint Congressional committee debated moving his body from Mount Vernon to a crypt in the [United States Capitol](/wiki/United_States_Capitol "United States Capitol"). Southern opposition was intense, antagonized by an ever-growing rift between North and South; many were concerned that Washington's remains could end up, in the words of Representative [Wiley Thompson](/wiki/Wiley_Thompson "Wiley Thompson"), on "a shore foreign to his native soil" if the country became divided, and Washington's remains stayed in Mount Vernon.[[265]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoorstin2010349–350-277) On October 7, 1837, Washington's remains, still in the original lead coffin, were placed within a marble [sarcophagus](/wiki/Sarcophagus "Sarcophagus") designed by [William Strickland](/wiki/William_Strickland_(architect) "William Strickland (architect)") and constructed by John Struthers.[[266]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECostello2021182Carlson2016chapter_1-278)

## Philosophy and views

### Slavery

Main article: [George Washington and slavery](/wiki/George_Washington_and_slavery "George Washington and slavery")

Further information: [Slavery in the colonial United States](/wiki/Slavery_in_the_colonial_United_States "Slavery in the colonial United States"), [Slavery in the United States](/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States "Slavery in the United States"), and [Abolitionism in the United States](/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States "Abolitionism in the United States")

During Washington's lifetime at least 577 slaves lived and worked at Mount Vernon.[[267]](#cite_note-Mount_Vernon_Slaves-279)[[268]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000279Ellis200445-280) He inherited some, gained control of 84 [dower](/wiki/Dowry "Dowry") slaves upon his marriage to Martha, and purchased at least 71 slaves between 1752 and 1773.[[269]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000279–280Morgan2005405,_407_n7Hirschfeld199712-281) From 1786, he rented slaves as part of an agreement regarding a neighboring estate; they totaled 40 in 1799.[[270]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirschfeld199719-282)

Slavery was [deeply ingrained](/wiki/History_of_slavery_in_Virginia "History of slavery in Virginia") in the economic and social fabric of the [Colony of Virginia](/wiki/Colony_of_Virginia "Colony of Virginia").[[271]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenriques2006146-283)[[272]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillcoxArnstein198841–42-284) Prior to the Revolutionary War, Washington's views on slavery matched those of most Virginia [planters](/wiki/Planter_class "Planter class") of the time: "his principal interest was still their contribution to the economic life of the plantation."[[273]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwohig2001116-285) Beginning in the 1760s, however, Washington gradually grew to oppose it. His first doubts were prompted by his choice to transition from tobacco to grain crops, which left him with a costly surplus of slaves, causing him to question the system's economic efficiency.[[274]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2005413-286) In a 1778 letter to [Lund Washington](/wiki/Lund_Washington "Lund Washington"), he made clear his desire "to get quit of Negroes".[[275]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2005416–417-287)

His growing disillusionment with the institution was spurred by the principles of the Revolution and revolutionary friends such as Lafayette and Hamilton.[[276]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwohig2001121Morgan2005426-288) Most historians agree the Revolution was central to the evolution of Washington's attitudes;[[277]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFurstenberg2011260-289) Kenneth Morgan writes that after 1783, "[Washington] began to express inner tensions about the problem of slavery more frequently, though always in private".[[278]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000299-290) As president, he remained publicly silent on the topic, believing it was a nationally divisive issue that could undermine the union.[[279]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2004202Twohig2001126-291) He gave moral support to a plan proposed by Lafayette to purchase land and free slaves to work on it, but chose not to participate in the experiment.[[280]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwohig2001-292) Washington privately expressed support for emancipation to prominent Methodists [Thomas Coke](/wiki/Thomas_Coke_(bishop) "Thomas Coke (bishop)") and [Francis Asbury](/wiki/Francis_Asbury "Francis Asbury") in 1785 but declined to sign their petition.[[281]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2000292-293) In personal correspondence the next year, he made clear his desire to see the institution of slavery ended by a gradual legislative process, a view that correlated with the mainstream antislavery literature published in the 1780s.[[282]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2005418–419Furstenberg2011273–274,_284–285-294) Washington emancipated 123 or 124 slaves, which was highly unusual among the large slave-holding Virginians during the Revolutionary era.[[283]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan20051404–405Wiencek2003352–35Hirschfeld199720-295) However, he remained dependent on slave labor to work his farms.[[284]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwohig2001122–123Morgan2000283,_289-296)

Based on his private papers and on accounts from his contemporaries, Washington slowly developed a cautious sympathy toward abolitionism that ended with his will [freeing](/wiki/Manumission "Manumission") his long-time valet [Billy Lee](/wiki/William_Lee_(valet) "William Lee (valet)"), and freeing the rest of his personally owned slaves outright upon Martha's death.[[285]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHirschfeld19973,_108,_209Morgan200029-297) On January 1, 1801, one year after George Washington's death, Martha Washington signed an order to free his slaves. Many of them were reluctant to leave; others refused to abandon spouses or children still held as dower slaves by the Custis estate.[[286]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010802-298) Following Washington's instructions in his will, funds were used to feed and clothe the young, aged, and infirm slaves until the early 1830s.[[287]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010815-299)[[31]](#cite_note-anb-34)

### Religious and spiritual views

Main article: [Religious views of George Washington](/wiki/Religious_views_of_George_Washington "Religious views of George Washington")

Washington was baptized as an infant in April 1732 and was a devoted member of the [Anglican Church](/wiki/Anglican_Church "Anglican Church").[[288]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow20106Morrison2009136Alden19962,_26Randall199717-300) He served for more than 20 years as a [vestryman](/wiki/Vestryman "Vestryman") and churchwarden at the [Fairfax](/wiki/Fairfax_Parish,_Virginia "Fairfax Parish, Virginia") and [Truro](/wiki/Truro_Parish,_Virginia "Truro Parish, Virginia") parishes in Virginia.[[289]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010130Thompson200840-301) He privately prayed and read the Bible daily, and publicly encouraged prayer.[[290]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrazer2012198–199Chernow2010119,_132Vicchio201927NovakNovak2007xvi-302) He may have taken [communion](/wiki/Eucharist "Eucharist") regularly prior to the Revolution, but he did not do so afterwards.[[291]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131,_470Holmes200662Frazer2012201–203-303)

Washington referred to God in [American Enlightenment](/wiki/American_Enlightenment "American Enlightenment") terms, including *[Providence](/wiki/Divine_providence "Divine providence")*, the *Almighty*, and the *Divine Author*.[[292]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall1997Vicchio2019101-304) He believed in a divine power who watched over battlefields, influenced the outcome of war, protected his life, and was involved in American politics and specifically in the creation of the United States.[[293]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010NovakNovak2007152-305) Chernow has argued that Washington avoided evangelistic Christianity, hellfire-and-brimstone speech, and anything inclined to "flaunt his religiosity", saying that he "never used his religion as a device for partisan purposes or in official undertakings".[[294]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131–132-306) At the same time, Washington frequently quoted from or paraphrased the Bible, and often referred to the Anglican *[Book of Common Prayer](/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer "Book of Common Prayer")*.[[295]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131–132Morrison2009136-307)

While president, Washington acknowledged major religious sects, gave speeches on religious toleration, and opposed [state religion](/wiki/State_religion "State religion").[[296]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010131Vicchio201960-308) He adopted the ideas, values, and modes of thinking of the Enlightenment,[[297]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood2001313-309) but he harbored no contempt for organized Christianity and its clergy.[[297]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood2001313-309) In 1793, speaking to members of the [New Church](/wiki/The_New_Church_(Swedenborgian) "The New Church (Swedenborgian)") in [Baltimore](/wiki/Baltimore "Baltimore"), Washington said, "We have abundant reason to rejoice that in this Land the light of truth and reason has triumphed over the power of bigotry and superstition."[[298]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTENovakNovak2007117,_n._52-310)

[Freemasonry](/wiki/Freemasonry "Freemasonry") was a widely accepted institution in the late 18th century, known for advocating moral teachings.[[299]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010132,_500Morrison2009136Stavish2007XIX,_XXITabbert20222–3-311) American [Masonic lodges](/wiki/Masonic_lodge "Masonic lodge") did not share the anti-clerical views of the [controversial European lodges](/wiki/Anticlericalism_and_Freemasonry "Anticlericalism and Freemasonry").[[300]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201027,_704-312) A Masonic lodge was established in [Fredericksburg, Virginia](/wiki/Fredericksburg,_Virginia "Fredericksburg, Virginia"), in September 1752, and Washington was initiated two months later at the age of 20 as one of its first Entered Apprentices. Within a year, he progressed through its ranks to become a Master Mason.[[259]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall199767Chernow201027-271) In 1777, he was recommended for the office of [Grand Master](/wiki/Grand_Master_(Freemasonry) "Grand Master (Freemasonry)") of the newly established [Grand Lodge of Virginia](/wiki/Grand_Lodge_of_Virginia "Grand Lodge of Virginia"); sources differ as to whether he declined or was never asked, but he did not assume the role.[[301]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETabbert202258–59-313) He served as the charter [Master](/wiki/Masonic_lodge_officer#Worshipful_Master "Masonic lodge officer") of Alexandria Masonic lodge No. 22 in 1788–1789.[[302]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETabbert2022103-314)

## Personal life

Washington's 1751 bout with smallpox may have rendered him sterile, though Chernow notes that it is possible Martha "sustained injury during the birth of Patsy, her final child, making additional births impossible".[[303]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010103-315) The couple lamented not having any children together.[[304]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010103Flexner197442–43-316) The two raised Martha's children [John Parke Custis](/wiki/John_Parke_Custis "John Parke Custis") (Jacky) and [Martha Parke Custis](/wiki/Martha_Parke_Custis "Martha Parke Custis") (Patsy), and later Jacky's two youngest children [Eleanor Parke Custis](/wiki/Eleanor_Parke_Custis_Lewis "Eleanor Parke Custis Lewis") (Nelly) and [George Washington Parke Custis](/wiki/George_Washington_Parke_Custis "George Washington Parke Custis") (Washy), and supported numerous nieces and nephews.[[305]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010101,_463-317) Some descendants of [West Ford](/wiki/West_Ford_(slave) "West Ford (slave)"), a slave of Washington's younger brother [John Augustine Washington](/wiki/John_Augustine_Washington "John Augustine Washington"), maintain (based on family [oral history](/wiki/Oral_history "Oral history")) that Ford was fathered by George Washington, though this paternity has been disputed.[[306]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010492–493Wiencek2003291–310-318)

Washington was somewhat reserved in personality, although he was known for having a strong presence. He made speeches and announcements when required, but he was not a noted orator nor debater.[[307]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200216Randall199734,_436Chernow201029–30-319) He drank alcohol in moderation but was morally opposed to excessive drinking, smoking tobacco, gambling, and profanity.[[308]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010134-320) He was taller than most of his contemporaries;[[309]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling200216-321) accounts of his height vary from 6 ft (1.83 m) to 6 ft 3.5 in (1.92 m).[[310]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201029-322) He was known for his strength.[[311]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010123–125-323) He had grey-blue eyes and long reddish-brown hair.[[312]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowlan201426-324) He did not wear a [powdered wig](/wiki/Wig#19th_and_20th_centuries "Wig"); instead he wore his hair curled, powdered, and tied in a [queue](/wiki/Queue_(hairstyle)#Other_queues "Queue (hairstyle)") in the [fashion of the day](/wiki/1775%E2%80%931795_in_Western_fashion#Men's_fashion "1775–1795 in Western fashion").[[313]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201030-325)[[314]](#cite_note-326)

Washington suffered from severe [tooth decay](/wiki/Tooth_decay "Tooth decay") and ultimately lost all of [his teeth](/wiki/George_Washington%27s_teeth "George Washington's teeth") except one. He had several sets of false teeth during his presidency. Contrary to common lore, these were not made of wood, but of metal, [ivory](/wiki/Ivory "Ivory"), bone, animal teeth, and human teeth possibly obtained from slaves.[[315]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMackowiak2021-327)[[316]](#cite_note-328) His dental problems left him in constant pain, which he treated with [laudanum](/wiki/Laudanum "Laudanum").[[317]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow201030,_290,_437–439,_642–643-329) He also experienced a painful growth in his thigh early in his first presidential term, followed by a life-threatening bout of [pneumonia](/wiki/Pneumonia "Pneumonia") in 1790 from which he never fully recovered.[[318]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhodehamel201710:_)

Washington was a talented equestrian. Jefferson described him as "the best horseman of his age".[[319]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010124-331) He collected thoroughbreds at Mount Vernon; his two favorite horses were [Blueskin](/wiki/Blueskin_(horse) "Blueskin (horse)") and [Nelson](/wiki/Nelson_(horse) "Nelson (horse)").[[320]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010124,_469-332)

## Legacy

Further information: [Legacy of George Washington](/wiki/Legacy_of_George_Washington "Legacy of George Washington") and [Historical rankings of presidents of the United States](/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_presidents_of_the_United_States "Historical rankings of presidents of the United States")

See also: [Cultural depictions of George Washington](/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_George_Washington "Cultural depictions of George Washington")

Washington is one of the most influential figures in American history.[[321]](#cite_note-333) Virginia's Governor [Henry Lee III](/wiki/Henry_Lee_III "Henry Lee III") [eulogized him](https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/The_Father_of_His_Country "s:The Father of His Country") as "first in war, first in peace, and first in the hearts of his countrymen".[[31]](#cite_note-anb-34) Polls have consistently placed Washington among the highest-ranked of presidents.[[322]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurrayBlessing19947–9,_15-334)[[323]](#cite_note-335)[[324]](#cite_note-336)

Washington became an international symbol for liberation and nationalism as the leader of the first successful revolution against a colonial empire.[[325]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunliffe195824–26-337) In 1879, Congress proclaimed [Washington's Birthday](/wiki/Presidents%27_Day "Presidents' Day") to be a federal holiday.[[326]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling20094-338) In 1976, he was posthumously appointed General of the Armies of the United States during the [American Bicentennial](/wiki/United_States_Bicentennial "United States Bicentennial"). President [Gerald Ford](/wiki/Gerald_Ford "Gerald Ford") stated that Washington would "rank first among all officers of the Army, past and present".[[m]](#cite_note-340)[[328]](#cite_note-341) On March 13, 1978, Washington was officially promoted by the Army.[[329]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKleber1978-342)

In 1809, [Mason Locke Weems](/wiki/Mason_Locke_Weems "Mason Locke Weems") wrote a [hagiographic](/wiki/Hagiography "Hagiography") biography to honor Washington.[[330]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeems191822-343) Chernow maintains that Weems attempted to humanize Washington, inspire "patriotism and morality", and foster "enduring myths", such as that of [Washington's refusal to lie about damaging his father's cherry tree](/wiki/Mason_Locke_Weems#Cherry-tree_anecdote "Mason Locke Weems").[[331]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010813–814Levy20136,_217Weems191822-344)[[332]](#cite_note-345) Weems' accounts have never been proven or disproven.[[333]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevy20136-346)

In the 21st century, Washington's reputation has been critically scrutinized. The historian John Ferling maintains that Washington remains the only founder and president ever to be referred to as "godlike", and points out that his character has been the most scrutinized by historians.[[334]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling2009xviii–xix-347) The author [David Hackett Fischer](/wiki/David_Hackett_Fischer "David Hackett Fischer") defined Washington's character as "integrity, self-discipline, courage, absolute honesty, resolve, and decision, but also forbearance, decency, and respect for others".[[335]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischer2004446-348)

Washington's legacy with Native Americans is mixed. Chernow describes Washington as always trying to be even-handed in dealing with Indigenous peoples, hoping they would abandon their itinerant hunting life and adapt to fixed agricultural communities in the manner of White settlers. He also maintains that Washington never advocated outright confiscation of tribal land or the forcible removal of tribes.[[336]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010666-349) By contrast, [Colin G. Calloway](/wiki/Colin_G._Calloway "Colin G. Calloway") wrote that "Washington had a lifelong obsession with getting Indian land, either for himself or for his nation, and initiated policies and campaigns that had devastating effects in Indian country."[[337]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECalloway201838-350) He stated:

> The growth of the nation demanded the dispossession of Indian people... But if Indians refused and resisted, as they often did, he felt he had no choice but to "extirpate" them and that the expeditions he sent to destroy Indian towns were therefore entirely justified.[[338]](#cite_note-351)

Along with other Founding Fathers, Washington has been criticized for holding enslaved people. Though he expressed the desire to see the abolition of slavery through legislation, he did not initiate or support any initiatives for bringing about its end. This has led to calls to remove his name from public buildings and his statue from public spaces.[[339]](#cite_note-352)[[340]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan2005419,_422Twohig2001-353)

Washington's [presidential library](/wiki/Presidential_library "Presidential library") is housed at Mount Vernon,[[341]](#cite_note-354) which is now a [National Historic Landmark](/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark "National Historic Landmark").[[342]](#cite_note-355) His papers are held by the [Library of Congress](/wiki/Library_of_Congress "Library of Congress").[[343]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowlan201456-356)

### Namesakes and monuments

Further information: [List of memorials to George Washington](/wiki/List_of_memorials_to_George_Washington "List of memorials to George Washington") and [List of statues of George Washington](/wiki/List_of_statues_of_George_Washington "List of statues of George Washington")

Many places and monuments have been named in honor of Washington, including the capital city of [Washington, D.C.](/wiki/Washington,_D.C. "Washington, D.C."), and the state of [Washington](/wiki/Washington_(state) "Washington (state)").[[344]](#cite_note-357) On February 21, 1885, the [Washington Monument](/wiki/Washington_Monument "Washington Monument") was dedicated, a 555-foot (169 m) marble [obelisk](/wiki/Obelisk "Obelisk") on the [National Mall](/wiki/National_Mall "National Mall") in Washington, D.C.[[345]](#cite_note-358)[[346]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowlan201462-359)

Washington appears as one of four presidents on the *Shrine of Democracy*, a colossal sculpture by [Gutzon Borglum](/wiki/Gutzon_Borglum "Gutzon Borglum") on [Mount Rushmore](/wiki/Mount_Rushmore "Mount Rushmore") in [South Dakota](/wiki/South_Dakota "South Dakota").[[347]](#cite_note-360)[[346]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowlan201462-359) The [George Washington Bridge](/wiki/George_Washington_Bridge "George Washington Bridge"), opened in 1931, connects New York City to [New Jersey](/wiki/New_Jersey "New Jersey").[[348]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTERockland202071-361) A number of [secondary schools and universities](/wiki/Washington_(disambiguation)#Education "Washington (disambiguation)") are named in honor of Washington, including [George Washington University](/wiki/George_Washington_University "George Washington University") and [Washington University in St. Louis](/wiki/Washington_University_in_St._Louis "Washington University in St. Louis").[[349]](#cite_note-362)[[350]](#cite_note-363)

He appears on contemporary United States currency, including the [one-dollar bill](/wiki/United_States_one-dollar_bill "United States one-dollar bill"), a [Presidential one-dollar coin](/wiki/Presidential_dollar_coins "Presidential dollar coins") and the [quarter-dollar coin](/wiki/Quarter_(United_States_coin) "Quarter (United States coin)") (the [Washington quarter](/wiki/Washington_quarter "Washington quarter")).[[351]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETschachler2020-364)[[352]](#cite_note-365) Washington was pictured on the nation's [first postage stamp](/wiki/Postage_stamps_and_postal_history_of_the_United_States#First_national_postage_stamps "Postage stamps and postal history of the United States") in 1847, and has since appeared on more United States postage stamps than anyone else.[[353]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWest20148-366)

Washington issue of 1862

[Washington–Franklin](/wiki/Washington%E2%80%93Franklin_Issues "Washington–Franklin Issues") issue of 1917

Washington [quarter dollar](/wiki/Quarter_(United_States_coin) "Quarter (United States coin)")

Washington [Presidential one-dollar coin](/wiki/Presidential_dollar_coins "Presidential dollar coins")

Washington on the 2009 [dollar bill](/wiki/United_States_one-dollar_bill "United States one-dollar bill")

## Notes

1. ^ [***a***](#cite_ref-fn1_1-0) [***b***](#cite_ref-fn1_1-1) [***c***](#cite_ref-fn1_1-2) Contemporaneous records used the [Old Style](/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates "Old Style and New Style dates") Julian calendar and the [Annunciation Style](/wiki/New_Year#Historical_European_new_year_dates "New Year") of enumerating years, recording his birth as February 11, 1731. The British [Calendar (New Style) Act 1750](/wiki/Calendar_(New_Style)_Act_1750 "Calendar (New Style) Act 1750") implemented in 1752 altered the official British dating method to the Gregorian calendar with the start of the year on January 1 (it had been March 25). These changes resulted in dates being moved forward 11 days and an advance of one year for those between January 1 and March 25. For a further explanation, see [Old Style and New Style dates](/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates "Old Style and New Style dates").[[2]](#cite_note-3)
2. **[^](#cite_ref-14)** The college's charter gave it the authority to appoint Virginia county surveyors. There is no evidence that Washington actually attended classes there.[[12]](#cite_note-professional-surveys-13)
3. **[^](#cite_ref-28)** The word "Indian" was used at the time to describe the [Indigenous peoples of the Americas](/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas "Indigenous peoples of the Americas").[[25]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECresswell2010222-27)
4. **[^](#cite_ref-56)** He had been defeated in his campaigns for the seat in 1755 and 1757.[[52]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMisencik2014176-55)
5. **[^](#cite_ref-60)** In a letter of September 20, 1765, Washington protested to "Robert Cary & Co." regarding the low prices he received for his tobacco and the inflated prices he was forced to pay on second-rate goods from London.[[55]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010138Ferling200968-59)
6. **[^](#cite_ref-75)** Other reasons Washington was perceived as the best choice for the commander role, according to Chernow, included his "superior presence, infinitely better judgment, more political cunning, and unmatched gravitas... he had the perfect temperament for leadership."[[69]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010185-74)
7. **[^](#cite_ref-94)** On January 24, 1776, Congressional delegate [Edward Rutledge](/wiki/Edward_Rutledge "Edward Rutledge"), echoing General George Washington's own concerns, suggested that a war office similar to Great Britain's be established.[[86]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright198389-92) Organization of the [Board of War](/wiki/Board_of_War "Board of War") underwent several significant changes after its inception in 1776.[[87]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright1983121f-93)
8. **[^](#cite_ref-123)** See, for example, Todd W. Braisted, *Grand Forage 1778*, Westholme Publishing, 2016.
9. **[^](#cite_ref-159)** Thomas Jefferson praised Washington for his "moderation and virtue" in relinquishing command. Reportedly, upon being informed of Washington's plans by painter [Benjamin West](/wiki/Benjamin_West "Benjamin West"), King George III remarked: "If he does that, he will be the greatest man in the world."[[150]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2010454Taylor2016319–320-158)
10. **[^](#cite_ref-188)** There has been debate over whether Washington added "so help me God" to the end of the oath.[[178]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenriques20203:_)
11. **[^](#cite_ref-265)** The first account of Washington's death was written by doctors Craik and Brown, published in *The Times* of [Alexandria](/wiki/Alexandria,_Virginia "Alexandria, Virginia") five days after his death. The complete text can be found in *The Eclectic Medical Journal* (1858).[[254]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewtonFreemanBickley1858273–274-264)
12. **[^](#cite_ref-269)** Modern medical experts who blamed medical malpractice include Morens and Wallenborn in 1999,[[255]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorens19991845–1849Chernow2010809-266)[[256]](#cite_note-267) and Cheatham in 2008.[[257]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECheatham2008-268)
13. **[^](#cite_ref-340)** In *Portraits & Biographical Sketches of the United States Army's Senior Officer*, William Gardner Bell states that Washington was recalled to military service from his retirement in 1798, and "Congress passed legislation that would have made him General of the Armies of the United States, but his services were not required in the field, and the appointment was not made until the Bicentennial in 1976 when it was bestowed posthumously as a commemorative honor."[[327]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell199252,_66-339)
